<h1 class="firstHeading mw-first-heading" id="firstHeading"><span class="mw-page-title-main">Quran</span></h1>
<div class="vector-body" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"></div>
<div id="contentSub2"></div>
<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#searchInput">Jump to search</a>
<div class="mw-body-content mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Foundational Islamic religious text</div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">This article is about the central religious text of Islam. For other uses, see <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Quran_(disambiguation)" title="Quran (disambiguation)">Quran (disambiguation)</a>.</div>
<p class="mw-empty-elt">
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1066479718">.mw-parser-output .infobox-subbox{padding:0;border:none;margin:-3px;width:auto;min-width:100%;font-size:100%;clear:none;float:none;background-color:transparent}.mw-parser-output .infobox-3cols-child{margin:auto}.mw-parser-output .infobox .navbar{font-size:100%}body.skin-minerva .mw-parser-output .infobox-header,body.skin-minerva .mw-parser-output .infobox-subheader,body.skin-minerva .mw-parser-output .infobox-above,body.skin-minerva .mw-parser-output .infobox-title,body.skin-minerva .mw-parser-output .infobox-image,body.skin-minerva .mw-parser-output .infobox-full-data,body.skin-minerva .mw-parser-output .infobox-below{text-align:center}</style><table class="infobox vcard" style="border-spacing:2px;"><tbody><tr><th class="infobox-above fn org" colspan="2" style="background: #9BE89B; color: #000000;"><b>Full text</b></th></tr><tr><td class="infobox-full-data" colspan="2"><img alt="" data-file-height="430" data-file-width="410" decoding="async" height="17" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Wikisource-logo.svg/16px-Wikisource-logo.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Wikisource-logo.svg/24px-Wikisource-logo.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Wikisource-logo.svg/32px-Wikisource-logo.svg.png 2x" width="16"/><i><span title="Arabic-language text"><span lang="ar"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/ar:%D8%A7%D9%84%D9%82%D8%B1%D8%A2%D9%86_%D8%A7%D9%84%D9%83%D8%B1%D9%8A%D9%85" title="s:ar:Ø§ÙÙØ±Ø¢Ù Ø§ÙÙØ±ÙÙ">The Quran</a></span></span></i> at Arabic <a href="/wiki/Wikisource" title="Wikisource">Wikisource</a></td></tr><tr><td class="infobox-full-data" colspan="2"><img alt="" data-file-height="430" data-file-width="410" decoding="async" height="17" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Wikisource-logo.svg/16px-Wikisource-logo.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Wikisource-logo.svg/24px-Wikisource-logo.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Wikisource-logo.svg/32px-Wikisource-logo.svg.png 2x" width="16"/> <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/Quran" title="wikisource:Quran">Quran</a> at <a href="/wiki/Wikisource" title="Wikisource"> English Wikisource</a></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>The <b>Quran</b> (<span class="rt-commentedText nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt" lang="en-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="'k' in 'kind'">k</span><span title="/Êr/: 'our' in 'courier'">Êr</span><span title="/Ë/: primary stress follows">Ë</span><span title="/ÉË/: 'a' in 'father'">ÉË</span><span title="'n' in 'nigh'">n</span></span>/</a></span></span>, <a href="/wiki/Help:Pronunciation_respelling_key" title="Help:Pronunciation respelling key"><i title="English pronunciation respelling">kuurr-<span style="font-size:90%">AHN</span></i></a>;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[i]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Arabic" title="Arabic">Arabic</a>: <style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1097707674">.mw-parser-output .script-arabic{font-family:"SF Arabic",Scheherazade,Lateef,LateefGR,Amiri,"Noto Naskh Arabic","Droid Arabic Naskh","Scheherazade New","Microsoft Uighur","Sakkal Majalla",Harmattan,"Arabic Typesetting","Arabic Transparent","Times New Roman",Arial,Calibri,"Microsoft Sans Serif","Segoe UI",serif,sans-serif;font-weight:normal}</style><span class="script-arabic script-Arab" dir="rtl" style="font-size: 125%;"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1097707674" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="script-arabic script-Arab" dir="rtl" style="font-size: 125%;">Ø§ÙÙØ±Ø¢Ù</span>â</span>â <span title="American Library Association â Library of Congress transliteration"><i lang="ar-Latn">al-QurÊ¾Än</i></span> <span class="nowrap"><small></small><span class="IPA" lang="ar-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Arabic" title="Help:IPA/Arabic">[alqurËÊaËn]</a></span></span>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[ii]</a></sup> 'the recitation'), also romanized <b>Qur'an</b> or <b>Koran</b>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[iii]</a></sup> is the central <a href="/wiki/Religious_text" title="Religious text">religious text</a> of <a href="/wiki/Islam" title="Islam">Islam</a>, believed by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Muslim" title="Muslim">Muslims</a> to be a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Revelation_in_Islam" title="Revelation in Islam">revelation</a> from <a href="/wiki/God_in_Islam" title="God in Islam">God</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Britannica_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-Britannica-14">[11]</a></sup> It is organized in 114 <a href="/wiki/Surah" title="Surah">chapters</a> (<small>pl.:</small> <big><span title="Arabic-language text"><span lang="ar"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1097707674" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="script-arabic script-Arab" dir="rtl" style="font-size: 125%;">Ø³ÙØ±</span>â</span></span></big> <span title="American Library Association â Library of Congress transliteration"><i lang="ar-Latn">suwar</i></span>, <small>sing.:</small> <big><span title="Arabic-language text"><span lang="ar"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1097707674" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="script-arabic script-Arab" dir="rtl" style="font-size: 125%;">Ø³ÙØ±Ø©</span>â</span></span></big> <span title="American Library Association â Library of Congress transliteration"><i lang="ar-Latn">sÅ«rah</i></span>), which consist of <a href="/wiki/%C4%80yah" title="Äyah">verses</a> (<small>pl.:</small> <big><span title="Arabic-language text"><span lang="ar"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1097707674" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="script-arabic script-Arab" dir="rtl" style="font-size: 125%;">Ø¢ÙØ§Øª</span>â</span></span></big> <span title="American Library Association â Library of Congress transliteration"><i lang="ar-Latn">Ê¾ÄyÄt</i></span>, <small>sing.:</small> <big><span title="Arabic-language text"><span lang="ar"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1097707674" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="script-arabic script-Arab" dir="rtl" style="font-size: 125%;">Ø¢ÙØ©</span>â</span></span></big> <span title="American Library Association â Library of Congress transliteration"><i lang="ar-Latn">Ê¾Äyah</i></span>, <small><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Construct_case" title="Construct case">cons.</a>:</small> <span title="American Library Association â Library of Congress transliteration"><i lang="ar-Latn">Ê¾Äyat</i></span>). In addition to its religious significance, it is widely regarded as the finest work in <a href="/wiki/Arabic_literature" title="Arabic literature">Arabic literature</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Esposito_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-Esposito-17">[14]</a></sup> and has significantly influenced the Arabic language.
</p><p>Muslims believe that the Quran was orally revealed by God to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Khatam_an-Nabiyyin" title="Khatam an-Nabiyyin">final prophet</a>, <a href="/wiki/Muhammad_in_Islam" title="Muhammad in Islam">Muhammad</a>, through the archangel <a href="/wiki/Gabriel" title="Gabriel">Gabriel</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lambert_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lambert-18">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Williams_&amp;_Drew_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-Williams_&amp;_Drew-19">[16]</a></sup> incrementally over a period of some 23 years, beginning in the month of <a href="/wiki/Ramadan" title="Ramadan">Ramadan</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[17]</a></sup> when Muhammad was 40; and concluding in 632, the year of his death.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Britannica_14-1"><a href="#cite_note-Britannica-14">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LivRlgP338_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-LivRlgP338-21">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[19]</a></sup> Muslims regard the Quran as Muhammad's most important <a href="/wiki/Miracle" title="Miracle">miracle</a>; a proof of his <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Prophethood" title="Prophethood">prophethood</a>;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[20]</a></sup> and the culmination of a series of divine messages starting with those revealed to <a href="/wiki/Adam" title="Adam">Adam</a>, including the <a href="/wiki/Torah" title="Torah">Torah</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Psalms" title="Psalms">Psalms</a> and the Gospel. The word <i>Quran</i> occurs some 70 times in the text itself, and other names and words are also said to refer to the Quran.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wheeler2002_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wheeler2002-24">[21]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Quran is thought by Muslims to be not simply divinely inspired, but the literal <a href="/wiki/Biblical_inspiration" title="Biblical inspiration">word of God</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Carroll-Q-H_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-Carroll-Q-H-25">[22]</a></sup> Muhammad did not write it as he did not know how to write. According to tradition, several of Muhammad's <a href="/wiki/Companions_of_the_Prophet" title="Companions of the Prophet">companions</a> served as scribes, recording the revelations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Donner-Companion_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-Donner-Companion-26">[23]</a></sup> Shortly after the prophet's death, the Quran was compiled by the companions, who had written down or memorized parts of it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jecampo_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-jecampo-27">[24]</a></sup> Caliph <a href="/wiki/Uthman" title="Uthman">Uthman</a> established a standard version, now known as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Uthmanic_codex" title="Uthmanic codex">Uthmanic codex</a>, which is generally considered the archetype of the Quran known today. There are, however, <a href="/wiki/Qira%CA%BCat" title="QiraÊ¼at">variant readings</a>, with mostly minor differences in meaning.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Donner-Companion_26-1"><a href="#cite_note-Donner-Companion-26">[23]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Quran assumes <a href="/wiki/Biblical_and_Quranic_narratives" title="Biblical and Quranic narratives">familiarity with major narratives</a> recounted in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Books_of_the_Bible" title="Books of the Bible">Biblical</a> and <a href="/wiki/Apocrypha" title="Apocrypha">apocryphal scriptures</a>. It summarizes some, dwells at length on others and, in some cases, presents alternative accounts and interpretations of events.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sanigosian_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-sanigosian-28">[25]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[26]</a></sup> The Quran describes itself as a book of guidance for mankind (<span class="plainlinks"> <a class="external text" href="https://quran.com/2?startingVerse=185" rel="nofollow">2:185</a></span>). It sometimes offers detailed accounts of specific historical events, and it often emphasizes the moral significance of an event over its narrative sequence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[27]</a></sup> Supplementing the Quran with explanations for some cryptic Quranic narratives, and rulings that also provide the basis for Islamic law in most denominations of Islam,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-handbook_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-handbook-31">[28]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[iv]</a></sup> are <i><a href="/wiki/Hadith" title="Hadith">hadiths</a></i>âoral and written traditions believed to describe words and actions of Muhammad.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[v]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-handbook_31-1"><a href="#cite_note-handbook-31">[28]</a></sup>  During prayers, the Quran is recited only in <a href="/wiki/Arabic" title="Arabic">Arabic</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[29]</a></sup>
</p><p>Someone who has memorized the entire Quran is called a <i><a href="/wiki/Hafiz_(Quran)" title="Hafiz (Quran)">hafiz</a></i>. A verse is sometimes recited with a special kind of <a href="/wiki/Elocution" title="Elocution">elocution</a> reserved for this purpose, called <i><a href="/wiki/Tajwid" title="Tajwid">tajwid</a></i>. During the month of <a href="/wiki/Ramadan" title="Ramadan">Ramadan</a>, Muslims typically complete the recitation of the whole Quran during <i><a href="/wiki/Tarawih" title="Tarawih">tarawih</a></i> prayers. In order to extrapolate the meaning of a particular Quranic verse, Muslims rely on <a href="/wiki/Exegesis" title="Exegesis">exegesis</a>, or commentary rather than a direct translation of the text.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[30]</a></sup>
</p>

</div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology_and_meaning">Etymology and meaning</span></h2>
<p>The word <span title="American Library Association â Library of Congress transliteration"><i lang="ar-Latn">qurÊ¼Än</i></span> appears about 70 times in the Quran itself, assuming various meanings. It is a <a href="/wiki/Verbal_noun" title="Verbal noun">verbal noun</a> (<a href="/wiki/Arabic_verbs#Verbal_noun_(maá¹£dar)" title="Arabic verbs"><span title="American Library Association â Library of Congress transliteration"><i lang="ar-Latn">maá¹£dar</i></span></a>) of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arabic_language" title="Arabic language">Arabic</a> verb <span title="American Library Association â Library of Congress transliteration"><i lang="ar-Latn">qaraÊ¼a</i></span> (<span title="Arabic-language text"><span lang="ar"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1097707674" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="script-arabic script-Arab" dir="rtl" style="font-size: 125%;">ÙØ±Ø£</span>â</span></span>) meaning 'he read' or 'he recited'. The <a href="/wiki/Syriac_language" title="Syriac language">Syriac</a> equivalent is <span title="Semitic languages romanization"><i lang="sem-Latn">qeryÄnÄ</i></span> (<span title="Classical Syriac-language text"><span dir="rtl" lang="syc">Ü©ÜªÜÜ¢Ü</span></span>), which refers to 'scripture reading' or 'lesson'.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Comprehensive_Aramaic_Lexicon_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-Comprehensive_Aramaic_Lexicon-36">[31]</a></sup> While some Western scholars consider the word to be derived from the Syriac, the majority of Muslim authorities hold the origin of the word is <span title="American Library Association â Library of Congress transliteration"><i lang="ar-Latn">qaraÊ¼a</i></span> itself.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Britannica_14-2"><a href="#cite_note-Britannica-14">[11]</a></sup> Regardless, it had become an Arabic term by Muhammad's lifetime.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Britannica_14-3"><a href="#cite_note-Britannica-14">[11]</a></sup> An important meaning of the word is the 'act of reciting', as reflected in an early Quranic passage: "It is for Us to collect it and to recite it (<span title="American Library Association â Library of Congress transliteration"><i lang="ar-Latn">qurÊ¼Änahu</i></span>)."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[32]</a></sup>
</p><p>In other verses, the word refers to 'an individual passage recited [by Muhammad]'. Its <a href="/wiki/Liturgy" title="Liturgy">liturgical</a> context is seen in a number of passages, for example: <i>"So when <span title="American Library Association â Library of Congress transliteration"><span lang="ar-Latn" style="font-style: normal">al-qurÊ¼Än</span></span> is recited, listen to it and keep silent."</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[33]</a></sup> The word may also assume the meaning of a codified scripture when mentioned with other scriptures such as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tawrat" title="Tawrat">Torah</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Injil" title="Injil">Gospel</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[34]</a></sup>
</p><p>The term also has closely related <a href="/wiki/Synonym" title="Synonym">synonyms</a> that are employed throughout the Quran. Each synonym possesses its own distinct meaning, but its use may converge with that of <span title="American Library Association â Library of Congress transliteration"><i lang="ar-Latn">qurÊ¼Än</i></span> in certain contexts. Such terms include <span title="Arabic-language romanization"><i lang="ar-Latn">kitÄb</i></span> ('book'), <span title="Arabic-language romanization"><i lang="ar-Latn"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ayah" title="Ayah">Äyah</a></i></span> ('sign'), and <span title="Arabic-language romanization"><i lang="ar-Latn"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sura" title="Sura">sÅ«rah</a></i></span> ('scripture'); the latter two terms also denote units of revelation. In the large majority of contexts, usually with a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Definite_article" title="Definite article">definite article</a> (<i>al-</i>), the word is referred to as the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wahy" title="Wahy">waá¸¥y</a></i> ('revelation'), that which has been "sent down" (<i><a href="/wiki/Tanzil" title="Tanzil">tanzÄ«l</a></i>) at intervals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[35]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[36]</a></sup> Other related words include: <span title="American Library Association â Library of Congress transliteration"><i lang="ar-Latn"><a href="/wiki/Dhikr" title="Dhikr">dhikr</a></i></span> ('remembrance'), used to refer to the Quran in the sense of a reminder and warning; and <span title="Arabic-language romanization"><i lang="ar-Latn"><a href="/wiki/Hikmah" title="Hikmah">á¸¥ikmah</a></i></span> ('wisdom'), sometimes referring to the revelation or part of it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Britannica_14-4"><a href="#cite_note-Britannica-14">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[vi]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Quran describes itself as 'the discernment' (<span title="Arabic-language romanization"><i lang="ar-Latn">al-furqÄn</i></span>), 'the mother book' (<span title="Arabic-language romanization"><i lang="ar-Latn">umm al-kitÄb</i></span>), 'the guide' (<span title="Arabic-language romanization"><i lang="ar-Latn">huda</i></span>), 'the wisdom' (<span title="Arabic-language romanization"><i lang="ar-Latn">hikmah</i></span>), 'the remembrance' (<span title="Arabic-language romanization"><i lang="ar-Latn">dhikr</i></span>), and 'the revelation' (<span title="Arabic-language romanization"><i lang="ar-Latn">tanzÄ«l</i></span>; 'something sent down', signifying the descent of an object from a higher place to lower place).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jaffer_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-Jaffer-43">[37]</a></sup> Another term is <span title="American Library Association â Library of Congress transliteration"><i lang="ar-Latn">al-kitÄb</i></span> ('The Book'), though it is also used in the Arabic language for other scriptures, such as the Torah and the Gospels. The term <i><a href="/wiki/Mus%27haf" title="Mus'haf">mus'haf</a></i> ('written work') is often used to refer to particular Quranic manuscripts but is also used in the Quran to identify earlier revealed books.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Britannica_14-5"><a href="#cite_note-Britannica-14">[11]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_Quran" title="History of the Quran">History of the Quran</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Prophetic_era">Prophetic era</span></h3>
<p>Islamic tradition relates that <a href="/wiki/Muhammad_in_Islam" title="Muhammad in Islam">Muhammad</a> received <a href="/wiki/Muhammad%27s_first_revelation" title="Muhammad's first revelation">his first revelation</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Jabal_al-Nour" title="Jabal al-Nour">Cave of Hira</a> during one of his isolated retreats to the mountains. Thereafter, he received revelations over a period of 23 years. According to <i><a href="/wiki/Hadith" title="Hadith">hadith</a></i> and <a href="/wiki/History_of_Islam" title="History of Islam">Muslim history</a>, after Muhammad <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hijra_(Islam)" title="Hijra (Islam)">immigrated to Medina</a> and formed an independent Muslim community, he ordered many of his <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sahabah" title="Sahabah">companions</a> to recite the Quran and to learn and teach the laws, which were revealed daily. It is related that some of the <a href="/wiki/Quraysh" title="Quraysh">Quraysh</a> who were taken prisoners at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Badr" title="Battle of Badr">Battle of Badr</a> regained their freedom after they had taught some of the Muslims the simple writing of the time. Thus a group of Muslims gradually became literate. As it was initially spoken, the Quran was recorded on tablets, bones, and the wide, flat ends of date palm fronds. Most suras were in use amongst early Muslims since they are mentioned in numerous sayings by both <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sunni" title="Sunni">Sunni</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shia" title="Shia">Shia</a> sources, relating Muhammad's use of the Quran as a call to Islam, the making of prayer and the manner of recitation. However, the Quran did not exist in book form at the time of Muhammad's death in 632.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[38]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-watt_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-watt-45">[39]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chi_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-chi-46">[40]</a></sup> There is agreement among scholars that Muhammad himself did not write down the revelation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-denffer_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-denffer-47">[41]</a></sup>
</p><p><i><a href="/wiki/Sahih_al-Bukhari" title="Sahih al-Bukhari">Sahih al-Bukhari</a></i> narrates Muhammad describing the revelations as, "Sometimes it is (revealed) like the ringing of a bell" and <a href="/wiki/Aisha" title="Aisha">Aisha</a> reported, "I saw the Prophet being inspired Divinely on a very cold day and noticed the sweat dropping from his forehead (as the Inspiration was over)."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[vii]</a></sup> Muhammad's first revelation, according to the Quran, was accompanied with a vision. The agent of revelation is mentioned as the "one mighty in power,"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[43]</a></sup> the one who "grew clear to view when he was on the uppermost horizon. Then he drew nigh and came down till he was (distant) two bows' length or even nearer."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-watt_45-1"><a href="#cite_note-watt-45">[39]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[44]</a></sup> The Islamic studies scholar Welch states in the <i><a href="/wiki/Encyclopaedia_of_Islam" title="Encyclopaedia of Islam">Encyclopaedia of Islam</a></i> that he believes the graphic descriptions of Muhammad's condition at these moments may be regarded as genuine, because he was severely disturbed after these revelations. According to Welch, these seizures would have been seen by those around him as convincing evidence for the superhuman origin of Muhammad's inspirations. However, Muhammad's critics accused him of being a possessed man, a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Clairvoyant" title="Clairvoyant">soothsayer</a> or a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Magician_(paranormal)" title="Magician (paranormal)">magician</a> since his experiences were similar to those claimed by such figures well known in <a href="/wiki/Pre-Islamic_Arabia" title="Pre-Islamic Arabia">ancient Arabia</a>. Welch additionally states that it remains uncertain whether these experiences occurred before or after Muhammad's initial claim of prophethood.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[45]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Iqra.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="416" data-file-width="550" decoding="async" height="151" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/Iqra.jpg/200px-Iqra.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/Iqra.jpg/300px-Iqra.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/Iqra.jpg/400px-Iqra.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Iqra.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Muhammad" title="Muhammad">Muhammad</a>'s first revelation, <a href="/wiki/Al-Alaq" title="Al-Alaq">Surah Al-Alaq</a>, later placed 96th in the Qur'anic regulations, in current writing style</div></div></div>
<p>The Quran describes Muhammad as "<span title="Arabic-language romanization"><i lang="ar-Latn">ummi</i></span>,"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[46]</a></sup> which is traditionally interpreted as 'illiterate', but the meaning is rather more complex. Medieval commentators such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Muhammad_ibn_Jarir_al-Tabari" title="Muhammad ibn Jarir al-Tabari">Al-Tabari</a> maintained that the term induced two meanings: first, the inability to read or write in general; second, the inexperience or ignorance of the previous books or scriptures (but they gave priority to the first meaning). Muhammad's illiteracy was taken as a sign of the genuineness of his prophethood. For example, according to <a href="/wiki/Fakhr_al-Din_al-Razi" title="Fakhr al-Din al-Razi">Fakhr al-Din al-Razi</a>, if Muhammad had mastered writing and reading he possibly would have been suspected of having studied the books of the ancestors. Some scholars such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/William_Montgomery_Watt" title="William Montgomery Watt">Watt</a> prefer the second meaning of <span title="Arabic-language romanization"><i lang="ar-Latn">ummi</i></span>âthey take it to indicate unfamiliarity with earlier sacred texts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-watt_45-2"><a href="#cite_note-watt-45">[39]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[47]</a></sup>
</p><p>The final verse of the Quran was revealed on the 18th of the Islamic month of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dhu_al-Hijjah" title="Dhu al-Hijjah">Dhu al-Hijjah</a> in the year 10 <a href="/wiki/Hijri_year" title="Hijri year">A.H.</a>, a date that roughly corresponds to February or March 632. The verse was revealed after <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Event_of_Ghadir_Khumm" title="Event of Ghadir Khumm">the Prophet finished delivering his sermon at Ghadir Khumm</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Compilation_and_preservation">Compilation and preservation</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Sanaa_manuscript" title="Sanaa manuscript">Sanaa manuscript</a> and <a href="/wiki/Birmingham_Quran_manuscript" title="Birmingham Quran manuscript">Birmingham Quran manuscript</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:292px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Birmingham_mushaf_Bismillah.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="182" data-file-width="1114" decoding="async" height="47" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Birmingham_mushaf_Bismillah.png/290px-Birmingham_mushaf_Bismillah.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Birmingham_mushaf_Bismillah.png/435px-Birmingham_mushaf_Bismillah.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Birmingham_mushaf_Bismillah.png/580px-Birmingham_mushaf_Bismillah.png 2x" width="290"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Birmingham_mushaf_Bismillah.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The basmala as written on the <a href="/wiki/Birmingham_Quran_manuscript" title="Birmingham Quran manuscript">Birmingham mus'haf manuscript</a>, the oldest surviving copy of the Qur'an. <a href="/wiki/Rasm" title="Rasm">Rasm</a>: "Ù®Ø³Ù Ø§ÙÙÙ Ø§ÙØ±Ø­ÙÚº Ø§ÙØ±Ø­ÙÙ".</div></div></div>
<p>Following Muhammad's death in 632, a number of his companions who knew the Quran by heart were killed in the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Yamama" title="Battle of Yamama">Battle of Yamama</a> by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Musaylimah" title="Musaylimah">Musaylimah</a>. The first caliph, <a href="/wiki/Abu_Bakr" title="Abu Bakr">Abu Bakr</a> (d. 634), subsequently decided to collect the book in one volume so that it could be preserved. <a href="/wiki/Zayd_ibn_Thabit" title="Zayd ibn Thabit">Zayd ibn Thabit</a> (d. 655) was the person to collect the Quran since "he used to write the Divine Inspiration for Allah's Apostle". Thus, a group of scribes, most importantly Zayd, collected the verses and produced a hand-written manuscript of the complete book. The manuscript according to Zayd remained with Abu Bakr until he died. Zayd's reaction to the task and the difficulties in collecting the Quranic material from parchments, palm-leaf stalks, thin stones (collectively known as <span title="Arabic-language romanization"><i lang="ar-Latn">suhuf</i></span>)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-demyth-62_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-demyth-62-55">[48]</a></sup> and from men who knew it by heart is recorded in earlier narratives. After Abu Bakr, in 644, <a href="/wiki/Hafsa_bint_Umar" title="Hafsa bint Umar">Hafsa bint Umar</a>, Muhammad's widow, was entrusted with the manuscript until the third caliph, Uthman ibn Affan, requested the standard copy from Hafsa bint Umar in about 650.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[49]</a></sup>
</p><p>In about 650, the third Caliph <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Uthman_ibn_Affan" title="Uthman ibn Affan">Uthman ibn Affan</a> (d. 656) began noticing slight differences in pronunciation of the Quran as Islam expanded beyond the <a href="/wiki/Arabian_Peninsula" title="Arabian Peninsula">Arabian Peninsula</a> into <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Persia" title="Persia">Persia</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Levant" title="Levant">Levant</a>, and North Africa. In order to preserve the sanctity of the text, he ordered a committee headed by Zayd to use Abu Bakr's copy and prepare a standard copy of the Quran.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tabatabae1988p99_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-Tabatabae1988p99-57">[50]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sbukhari1_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-sbukhari1-58">[51]</a></sup>  Thus, within 20 years of Muhammad's death, the Quran was committed to written form.  That text became the model from which copies were made and promulgated throughout the urban centers of the Muslim world, and other versions are believed to have been destroyed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tabatabae1988p99_57-1"><a href="#cite_note-Tabatabae1988p99-57">[50]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rippin_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-rippin-59">[52]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[53]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MCKVSI2000:117â124_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-MCKVSI2000:117â124-61">[54]</a></sup> The present form of the Quran text is accepted by Muslim scholars to be the original version compiled by Abu Bakr.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-watt_45-3"><a href="#cite_note-watt-45">[39]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chi_46-1"><a href="#cite_note-chi-46">[40]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[viii]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Quran_by_Imam_ali.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1936" data-file-width="2592" decoding="async" height="164" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/Quran_by_Imam_ali.JPG/220px-Quran_by_Imam_ali.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/Quran_by_Imam_ali.JPG/330px-Quran_by_Imam_ali.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/Quran_by_Imam_ali.JPG/440px-Quran_by_Imam_ali.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Quran_by_Imam_ali.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Quran â in <a href="/wiki/Mashhad" title="Mashhad">Mashhad</a>, Iran â said to be written by <a href="/wiki/Ali" title="Ali">Ali</a></div></div></div>
<p>According to <a href="/wiki/Shia_Islam" title="Shia Islam">Shia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ali" title="Ali">Ali ibn Abi Talib</a> (d. 661) compiled a complete version of the Quran shortly after Muhammad's death. The order of this text differed from that gathered later during Uthman's era in that this version had been collected in chronological order. Despite this, he made no objection against the standardized Quran and accepted the Quran in circulation. Other personal copies of the Quran might have existed including <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Abd_Allah_ibn_Mas%27ud" title="Abd Allah ibn Mas'ud">Ibn Mas'ud</a>'s and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ubay_ibn_Ka%27b" title="Ubay ibn Ka'b">Ubay ibn Ka'b</a>'s codex, none of which exist today.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Britannica_14-6"><a href="#cite_note-Britannica-14">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tabatabae1988p99_57-2"><a href="#cite_note-Tabatabae1988p99-57">[50]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-leaman_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-leaman-64">[56]</a></sup>
</p><p>
The Quran most likely existed in scattered written form during Muhammad's lifetime. Several sources indicate that during Muhammad's lifetime a large number of his companions had memorized the revelations. Early commentaries and Islamic historical sources support the above-mentioned understanding of the Quran's early development.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jecampo_27-1"><a href="#cite_note-jecampo-27">[24]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/University_of_Chicago" title="University of Chicago">University of Chicago</a> professor <a href="/wiki/Fred_Donner" title="Fred Donner">Fred Donner</a> states that:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[57]</a></sup></p><blockquote><p>[T]here was a very early attempt to establish a uniform consonantal text of the QurÊ¾Än from what was probably a wider and more varied group of related texts in early transmission.â¦ After the creation of this standardized canonical text, earlier authoritative texts were suppressed, and all extant manuscriptsâdespite their numerous variantsâseem to date to a time after this standard consonantal text was established.</p></blockquote><p>Although most variant readings of the text of the Quran have ceased to be transmitted, some still are.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-melchert2_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-melchert2-66">[58]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[59]</a></sup> There has been no <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Critical_text" title="Critical text">critical text</a> produced on which a scholarly reconstruction of the Quranic text could be based.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[ix]</a></sup> Historically, controversy over the Quran's content has rarely become an issue, although debates continue on the subject.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[61]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[x]</a></sup>
</p><div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sana%27a1_Stanford_%2707_recto.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1380" data-file-width="1077" decoding="async" height="256" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/Sana%27a1_Stanford_%2707_recto.jpg/200px-Sana%27a1_Stanford_%2707_recto.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/Sana%27a1_Stanford_%2707_recto.jpg/300px-Sana%27a1_Stanford_%2707_recto.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/Sana%27a1_Stanford_%2707_recto.jpg/400px-Sana%27a1_Stanford_%2707_recto.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Sana%27a1_Stanford_%2707_recto.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The right page of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sana%27a_manuscript" title="Sana'a manuscript">Stanford '07 binary manuscript</a>. The upper layer is verses 265â271 of the surah <a href="/wiki/Bakara" title="Bakara">Bakara</a>. The double-layer reveals the additions made on the first text of the Qur'an and the differences with today's Qur'an.</div></div></div>
<p>In 1972, in a mosque in the city of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sana%27a" title="Sana'a">Sana'a</a>, <a href="/wiki/Yemen" title="Yemen">Yemen</a>, manuscripts were discovered that were later proved to be the most ancient Quranic text known to exist at the time. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sana%27a_manuscript" title="Sana'a manuscript">Sana'a manuscripts</a> contain <a href="/wiki/Palimpsest" title="Palimpsest">palimpsests</a>, a manuscript page from which the text has been washed off to make the parchment reusable againâa practice which was common in ancient times due to the scarcity of writing material. However, the faint washed-off underlying text (<span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">scriptio inferior</i></span>) is still barely visible and believed to be "pre-Uthmanic" Quranic content, while the text written on top (<span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">scriptio superior</i></span>) is believed to belong to Uthmanic times.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jqs1_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-jqs1-72">[62]</a></sup> Studies using <a href="/wiki/Radiocarbon_dating" title="Radiocarbon dating">radiocarbon dating</a> indicate that the parchments are dated to the period before 671 CE with a 99 percent probability.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bergmann_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-bergmann-73">[63]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sadeghi_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-sadeghi-74">[64]</a></sup> The German scholar <a href="/wiki/Gerd_R._Puin" title="Gerd R. Puin">Gerd R. Puin</a> has been investigating these Quran fragments for years. His research team made 35,000 microfilm photographs of the manuscripts, which he dated to the early part of the 8th century. Puin has not published the entirety of his work, but noted unconventional verse orderings, minor textual variations, and rare styles of orthography. He also suggested that some of the parchments were palimpsests which had been reused. Puin believed that this implied an evolving text as opposed to a fixed one.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LESTER-1999_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-LESTER-1999-75">[65]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2015, <a href="/wiki/Birmingham_Quran_manuscript" title="Birmingham Quran manuscript">fragments of a very early Quran</a>, dating back to 1370 years earlier, were discovered in the library of the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Birmingham" title="University of Birmingham">University of Birmingham</a>, England. According to the tests carried out by the Oxford University Radiocarbon Accelerator Unit, "with a probability of more than 95%, the parchment was from between 568 and 645". The manuscript is written in <a href="/wiki/Hijazi_script" title="Hijazi script">Hijazi script</a>, an early form of written Arabic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-oldest_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-oldest-76">[66]</a></sup> This is possibly the earliest extant exemplar of the Quran, but as the tests allow a range of possible dates, it cannot be said with certainty which of the existing versions is the oldest.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-oldest_76-1"><a href="#cite_note-oldest-76">[66]</a></sup> Saudi scholar Saud al-Sarhan has expressed doubt over the age of the fragments as they contain dots and chapter separators that are believed to have originated later.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[67]</a></sup> However <a href="/wiki/Joseph_E._B._Lumbard" title="Joseph E. B. Lumbard">Joseph E. B. Lumbard</a> of <a href="/wiki/Brandeis_University" title="Brandeis University">Brandeis University</a> has written in the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Huffington_Post" title="Huffington Post">Huffington Post</a></i> in support of the dates proposed by the Birmingham scholars. Lumbard notes that the discovery of a Quranic text that may be confirmed by radiocarbon dating as having been written in the first decades of the Islamic era, while presenting a text substantially in conformity with that traditionally accepted, reinforces a growing academic consensus that many Western skeptical and 'revisionist' theories of Quranic origins are now untenable in the light of empirical findingsâwhereas, on the other hand, counterpart accounts of Quranic origins within classical Islamic traditions stand up well in the light of ongoing scientific discoveries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[68]</a></sup>
</p>

</tr><tr><td class="sidebar-below plainlist" style="padding-top:0.1em;font-weight:bold;line-height:1.5em;">
<ul><li><img alt="" class="noviewer" data-file-height="215" data-file-width="206" decoding="async" height="16" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/Allah-green.svg/15px-Allah-green.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/Allah-green.svg/23px-Allah-green.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/Allah-green.svg/31px-Allah-green.svg.png 2x" width="15"/>Â <a href="/wiki/Portal:Islam" title="Portal:Islam">Islam portal</a></li></ul></td></tr><tr><td class="sidebar-navbar" style="padding-right:0.2em;"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1063604349" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="navbar plainlinks hlist navbar-mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Islam" title="Template:Islam"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Islam" title="Template talk:Islam"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Islam&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Muslims believe the Quran to be God's final revelation to humanity, a work of divine guidance revealed to <a href="/wiki/Muhammad_in_Islam" title="Muhammad in Islam">Muhammad</a> through the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Holy_Spirit_(Islam)" title="Holy Spirit (Islam)">angel Gabriel</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LivRlgP338_21-1"><a href="#cite_note-LivRlgP338-21">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-79">[69]</a></sup>
</p><p>Revered by pious Muslims as "the holy of holies,"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AGI1954:74_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-AGI1954:74-80">[70]</a></sup> whose sound moves some to "tears and ecstasy",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-meanings-iii_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-meanings-iii-81">[71]</a></sup> it is the physical symbol of the faith, the text often used as a charm on occasions of birth, death, marriage.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2021)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Consequently,
</p>
<blockquote><p> It must never rest beneath other books, but always on top of them, one must never drink or smoke when it is being read aloud, and it must be listened to in silence. It is a talisman against disease and disaster.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AGI1954:74_80-1"><a href="#cite_note-AGI1954:74-80">[70]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-iWWINaM1995:105_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-iWWINaM1995:105-82">[72]</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<p>Traditionally great emphasis was put on children memorizing the 6,200+ verses of the Quran, those succeeding being honored with the title <a href="/wiki/Hafiz_(Quran)" title="Hafiz (Quran)">Hafiz</a>. "Millions and millions" of Muslims "refer to the Koran daily to explain their actions and to justify their aspirations,"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[xi]</a></sup> and in recent years many consider it the source of scientific knowledge.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Guessoum-2008_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-Guessoum-2008-85">[74]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SARDAR-2008_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-SARDAR-2008-86">[75]</a></sup>
</p><p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wahy" title="Wahy">Revelation</a> in Islamic and Quranic contexts means the act of God addressing an individual, conveying a message for a greater number of recipients. The process by which the divine message comes to the heart of a messenger of God is <i><a href="/wiki/Tanzil" title="Tanzil">tanzil</a></i> ('to send down') or <span title="Arabic-language romanization"><i lang="ar-Latn">nuzÅ«l</i></span> ('to come down'). As the Quran says, "With the truth we (God) have sent it down and with the truth it has come down."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[76]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Quran frequently asserts in its text that it is divinely ordained. Some verses in the Quran seem to imply that even those who do not speak Arabic would understand the Quran if it were recited to them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jenssen2001_88-0"><a href="#cite_note-jenssen2001-88">[77]</a></sup> The Quran refers to a written pre-text, "the preserved tablet," that records God's speech even before it was sent down.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tsonn_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-tsonn-89">[78]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[79]</a></sup>
</p><p>Muslims believe that the present wording of the Quran corresponds to that revealed to Muhammad, and according to their interpretation of Quran <span class="plainlinks"> <a class="external text" href="https://quran.com/15?startingVerse=9" rel="nofollow">15:9</a></span>, it is protected from corruption ("Indeed, it is We who sent down the Quran and indeed, We will be its guardian.").<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[80]</a></sup> Muslims consider the Quran to be a guide, a sign of the prophethood of Muhammad and the truth of the religion.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Shia_view_of_the_Quran" title="Shia view of the Quran">ShÄ«a believe that the Quran</a> was gathered and compiled by Muhammad during his lifetime, rather than being compiled by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Uthman_ibn_Affan" title="Uthman ibn Affan">Uthman ibn Affan</a>.  There are other differences in the way Shias interpret the text.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Shirazi01_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-Shirazi01-92">[81]</a></sup> Muslims do not agree over whether the Quran was created by God or is eternal and "uncreated."  Sunnis (who make up about 85â90% of Muslims) hold that the Quran is uncreatedâa doctrine that has been unchallenged among them for many centuries. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shia" title="Shia">Shia</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Twelver" title="Twelver">Twelvers</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Zaydi" title="Zaydi">Zaydi</a>, and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kharijite" title="Kharijite">Kharijites</a>âbelieve the Quran was created.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GlassÃ©-268_93-0"><a href="#cite_note-GlassÃ©-268-93">[82]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sufi" title="Sufi">Sufi</a> philosophers view the question as artificial or wrongly framed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-corbin1993_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-corbin1993-94">[83]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 10">:â10â</span></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability"><span title="Quotation needed from source to verify. (August 2017)">need quotation to verify</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Inimitability">Inimitability</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/I%27jaz" title="I'jaz">I'jaz</a></div>
<p>Inimitability of the Quran (or "<span title="Arabic-language romanization"><i lang="ar-Latn">I'jaz</i></span>") is the belief that no human speech can match the Quran in its content and form. The Quran is considered an inimitable miracle by Muslims, effective until the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Day_of_Resurrection" title="Day of Resurrection">Day of Resurrection</a>âand, thereby, the central proof granted to <a href="/wiki/Muhammad" title="Muhammad">Muhammad</a> in authentication of his prophetic status. The concept of inimitability originates in the Quran where in five different verses opponents are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Challenge_of_the_Quran" title="Challenge of the Quran">challenged to produce something like the Quran</a>: "If men and <a href="/wiki/Jinn" title="Jinn">jinn</a> banded together to produce the like of this Quran they would never produce its like not though they backed one another."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[84]</a></sup> From the ninth century, numerous works appeared which studied the Quran and examined its style and content. Medieval Muslim scholars including <a href="/wiki/Abd_al-Qahir_al-Jurjani" title="Abd al-Qahir al-Jurjani">al-Jurjani</a> (d. 1078) and <a href="/wiki/Al-Baqillani" title="Al-Baqillani">al-Baqillani</a> (d. 1013) have written treatises on the subject, discussed its various aspects, and used linguistic approaches to study the Quran. Others argue that the Quran contains noble ideas, has inner meanings, maintained its freshness through the ages and has caused great transformations at the individual level and in history. Some scholars state that the Quran contains scientific information that agrees with modern science. The doctrine of the miraculousness of the Quran is further emphasized by Muhammad's illiteracy since the unlettered prophet could not have been suspected of composing the Quran.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-leaman_64-1"><a href="#cite_note-leaman-64">[56]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sophia_96-0"><a href="#cite_note-sophia-96">[85]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="In_worship">In worship</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Salah" title="Salah">Salah</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:COLLECTIE_TROPENMUSEUM_Moslimmannen_tijdens_het_gebed_op_vrijdag_in_de_moskee_Tulehu_TMnr_20017952.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="452" data-file-width="700" decoding="async" height="142" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2e/COLLECTIE_TROPENMUSEUM_Moslimmannen_tijdens_het_gebed_op_vrijdag_in_de_moskee_Tulehu_TMnr_20017952.jpg/220px-COLLECTIE_TROPENMUSEUM_Moslimmannen_tijdens_het_gebed_op_vrijdag_in_de_moskee_Tulehu_TMnr_20017952.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2e/COLLECTIE_TROPENMUSEUM_Moslimmannen_tijdens_het_gebed_op_vrijdag_in_de_moskee_Tulehu_TMnr_20017952.jpg/330px-COLLECTIE_TROPENMUSEUM_Moslimmannen_tijdens_het_gebed_op_vrijdag_in_de_moskee_Tulehu_TMnr_20017952.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2e/COLLECTIE_TROPENMUSEUM_Moslimmannen_tijdens_het_gebed_op_vrijdag_in_de_moskee_Tulehu_TMnr_20017952.jpg/440px-COLLECTIE_TROPENMUSEUM_Moslimmannen_tijdens_het_gebed_op_vrijdag_in_de_moskee_Tulehu_TMnr_20017952.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:COLLECTIE_TROPENMUSEUM_Moslimmannen_tijdens_het_gebed_op_vrijdag_in_de_moskee_Tulehu_TMnr_20017952.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>While standing in prayers, worshipers recite the first chapter of the Quran, <a href="/wiki/Al-Fatiha" title="Al-Fatiha">al-Fatiha</a>, followed by any other section.</div></div></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Al-Fatiha" title="Al-Fatiha">first surah</a> of the Quran is repeated in daily prayers and on other occasions. This surah, which consists of seven verses, is the most often recited surah of the Quran:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Britannica_14-7"><a href="#cite_note-Britannica-14">[11]</a></sup>
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1109925960">.mw-parser-output .verse_translation .translated{padding-left:2em}@media only screen and (max-width:43.75em){.mw-parser-output .verse_translation.wrap_when_small td{display:block;padding-left:0}.mw-parser-output .verse_translation.wrap_when_small .translated{padding-left:0}}</style>
<table class="verse_translation" role="presentation" style="margin-left:1em;">
<tbody><tr style="vertical-align: top;">
<td><div class="poem" lang="ar" style="font-style:roman;text-align:right">
<p><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1097707674" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="script-arabic script-Arab" dir="rtl" style="font-size: 125%;">Ø¨ÙØ³ÙÙÙ Ù±ÙÙÙÙÙÙ Ù±ÙØ±ÙÙØ­ÙÙÙÙ°ÙÙ Ù±ÙØ±ÙÙØ­ÙÙÙÙ<br/>
Ù±ÙÙØ­ÙÙÙØ¯Ù ÙÙÙÙÙÙÙ Ø±ÙØ¨ÙÙ Ù±ÙÙØ¹ÙÙ°ÙÙÙÙÙÙÙ<br/>
Ù±ÙØ±ÙÙØ­ÙÙÙÙ°ÙÙ Ù±ÙØ±ÙÙØ­ÙÙÙÙ<br/>
ÙÙÙ°ÙÙÙÙ ÙÙÙÙÙÙ Ù±ÙØ¯ÙÙÙÙÙ<br/>
Ø¥ÙÙÙÙØ§ÙÙ ÙÙØ¹ÙØ¨ÙØ¯Ù ÙÙØ¥ÙÙÙÙØ§ÙÙ ÙÙØ³ÙØªÙØ¹ÙÙÙÙ<br/>
Ù±ÙÙØ¯ÙÙÙØ§ Ù±ÙØµÙÙØ±ÙÙ°Ø·Ù Ù±ÙÙÙÙØ³ÙØªÙÙÙÙÙÙ<br/>
ØµÙØ±ÙÙ°Ø·Ù Ù±ÙÙÙØ°ÙÙÙÙ Ø£ÙÙÙØ¹ÙÙÙØªÙ Ø¹ÙÙÙÙÙÙÙÙÙ ÙØ§Ù¥ ØºÙÙÙØ±Ù Ù±ÙÙÙÙØºÙØ¶ÙÙØ¨Ù Ø¹ÙÙÙÙÙÙÙÙÙ ÙÙÙÙØ§ Ù±ÙØ¶ÙÙØ¢ÙÙÙÙÙÙ</span>â
</p>
</div>
</td>
<td class="translated"><div class="poem">
<p>In the Name of Allah the Entirely Merciful, the Especially Merciful.<br/>
[All] praise is [due] to Allah, Lord of the worlds â<br/>
The Entirely Merciful, the Especially Merciful,<br/>
Sovereign of the Day of Recompense.<br/>
It is You we worship and You we ask for help<br/>
Guide us to the straight pathâ<br/>
The path of those upon whom You have bestowed favor, not of those who have evoked [Your] anger or of those who are astray.
</p>
</div>
</td></tr>
<tr style="vertical-align: top; font-size:90%">
<td>â Quran <a class="external text" href="https://quran.com/1?startingVerse=1" rel="nofollow">1:1-7</a>
</td>
<td style="padding-left: 2em;">â<i><a href="/wiki/Sahih_International" title="Sahih International">Sahih International</a></i> English translation
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Other sections of the Quran of choice are also read in daily prayers.
</p><p>Respect for the written text of the Quran is an important element of religious faith by many Muslims, and the Quran is treated with reverence. Based on tradition and a literal interpretation of Quran <span class="plainlinks"> <a class="external text" href="https://quran.com/56?startingVerse=79" rel="nofollow">56:79</a></span> ("none shall touch but those who are clean"), some Muslims believe that they must perform a ritual cleansing with water (<a href="/wiki/Wudu" title="Wudu">wudu</a> or <a href="/wiki/Ghusl" title="Ghusl">ghusl</a>) before touching a copy of the Quran, although this view is not universal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Britannica_14-8"><a href="#cite_note-Britannica-14">[11]</a></sup> Worn-out copies of the Quran are wrapped in a cloth and stored indefinitely in a safe place, buried in a mosque or a Muslim cemetery, or burned and the ashes buried or scattered over water.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[86]</a></sup>
</p><p>In Islam, most intellectual disciplines, including Islamic theology, <a href="/wiki/Islamic_philosophy" title="Islamic philosophy">philosophy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sufism" title="Sufism">mysticism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Fiqh" title="Fiqh">jurisprudence</a>, have been concerned with the Quran or have their foundation in its teachings.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Britannica_14-9"><a href="#cite_note-Britannica-14">[11]</a></sup> Muslims believe that the preaching or reading of the Quran is rewarded with divine rewards variously called <span title="Arabic-language romanization"><i lang="ar-Latn">ajr</i></span>, <i><a href="/wiki/Thawab" title="Thawab">thawab</a></i>, or <span title="Arabic-language romanization"><i lang="ar-Latn">hasanat</i></span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[87]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="In_Islamic_art">In Islamic art</span></h3>
<p>The Quran also inspired <a href="/wiki/Islamic_art" title="Islamic art">Islamic arts</a> and specifically the so-called Quranic arts of <a href="/wiki/Islamic_calligraphy" title="Islamic calligraphy">calligraphy</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_illumination" title="Ottoman illumination">illumination</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Britannica_14-10"><a href="#cite_note-Britannica-14">[11]</a></sup> The Quran is never decorated with figurative images, but many Qurans have been highly decorated with decorative patterns in the margins of the page, or between the lines or at the start of suras. Islamic verses appear in many other media, on buildings and on objects of all sizes, such as <a href="/wiki/Mosque_lamp" title="Mosque lamp">mosque lamps</a>, metal work, <a href="/wiki/Islamic_pottery" title="Islamic pottery">pottery</a> and single pages of calligraphy for <a href="/wiki/Muraqqa" title="Muraqqa">muraqqas</a> or albums.
</p>
