<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Text_and_arrangement">Text and arrangement</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Surah" title="Surah">Surah</a> and <a href="/wiki/%C4%80yah" title="Äyah">Äyah</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:232px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:FirstSurahKoran_(fragment).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="509" data-file-width="444" decoding="async" height="264" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/FirstSurahKoran_%28fragment%29.jpg/230px-FirstSurahKoran_%28fragment%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/FirstSurahKoran_%28fragment%29.jpg/345px-FirstSurahKoran_%28fragment%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/8/83/FirstSurahKoran_%28fragment%29.jpg 2x" width="230"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:FirstSurahKoran_(fragment).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>First sura of the Quran, <i><a href="/wiki/Al-Fatiha" title="Al-Fatiha">Al-Fatiha</a></i>, consisting of seven verses.</div></div></div>
<p>The Quran consists of 114 chapters of varying lengths, each known as a <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/S%C5%ABrah" title="SÅ«rah">sÅ«rah</a></i>. Chapters are classified as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Meccan_sura" title="Meccan sura">Meccan</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Medinan_sura" title="Medinan sura">Medinan</a>, depending on whether the verses were revealed before or after the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hijra_(Islam)" title="Hijra (Islam)">migration</a> of Muhammad to the city of Medina. However, a sÅ«rah classified as Medinan may contain Meccan verses in it and vice versa. SÅ«rah titles are derived from a name or quality discussed in the text, or from the first letters or words of the sÅ«rah. Chapters are not arranged in chronological order, rather the chapters appear to be arranged roughly in order of decreasing size. Some scholars argue the sÅ«rahs are arranged according to a certain pattern.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[88]</a></sup> Each sÅ«rah except the ninth starts with the <i><a href="/wiki/Basmala" title="Basmala">Bismillah</a></i> (<big><span title="Arabic-language text"><span lang="ar"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1097707674" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="script-arabic script-Arab" dir="rtl" style="font-size: 125%;">Ø¨ÙØ³ÙÙÙ Ù±ÙÙÙÙÙ°ÙÙ Ù±ÙØ±ÙÙØ­ÙÙÙÙ°ÙÙ Ù±ÙØ±ÙÙØ­ÙÙÙÙ</span>â</span></span></big>), an Arabic phrase meaning 'In the name of God.' There are, however, still 114 occurrences of the <i>Bismillah</i> in the Quran, due to its presence in <span class="plainlinks">Quran  <a class="external text" href="https://quran.com/27?startingVerse=30" rel="nofollow">27:30</a></span> as the opening of <a href="/wiki/Solomon_in_Islam" title="Solomon in Islam">Solomon</a>'s letter to the <a href="/wiki/Queen_of_Sheba" title="Queen of Sheba">Queen of Sheba</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[89]</a></sup>
</p><p>Each sÅ«rah consists of several verses, known as <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/%C4%80y%C4%81t" title="ÄyÄt">ÄyÄt</a></i>, which originally means a 'sign' or 'evidence' sent by God. The number of verses differs from sÅ«rah to sÅ«rah. An individual verse may be just a few letters or several lines. The total number of verses in the most popular <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hafs_Quran" title="Hafs Quran">Hafs Quran</a> is 6,236;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[xii]</a></sup> however, the number varies if the <i>bismillahs</i> are counted separately.
</p><p>In addition to and independent of the division into chapters, there are various ways of dividing the Quran into parts of approximately equal length for convenience in reading. The 30 <i><a href="/wiki/Juz%27" title="Juz'">juz'</a></i> (plural <span title="Arabic-language romanization"><i lang="ar-Latn">ajzÄÊ¼</i></span>) can be used to read through the entire Quran in a month. Some of these parts are known by namesâwhich are the first few words by which the <span title="Arabic-language romanization"><i lang="ar-Latn">juzÊ¼</i></span> begins. A <span title="Arabic-language romanization"><i lang="ar-Latn">juz'</i></span> is sometimes further divided into two <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hizb" title="Hizb">á¸¥izb</a></i> (plural <span title="Arabic-language romanization"><i lang="ar-Latn">aá¸¥zÄb</i></span>), and each <span title="Arabic-language romanization"><i lang="ar-Latn">hizb</i></span> subdivided into four <span title="Arabic-language romanization"><i lang="ar-Latn">rubÊ» al-ahzab</i></span>. The Quran is also divided into seven approximately equal parts, <i><a href="/wiki/Manzil" title="Manzil">manzil</a></i> (plural <span title="Arabic-language romanization"><i lang="ar-Latn">manÄzil</i></span>), for it to be recited in a week.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Britannica_14-11"><a href="#cite_note-Britannica-14">[11]</a></sup>
</p><p>A different structure is provided by semantic units resembling paragraphs and comprising roughly ten <span title="Arabic-language romanization"><i lang="ar-Latn">ÄyÄt</i></span> each. Such a section is called a <a href="/wiki/Ruku" title="Ruku"><i>rukÅ«`</i></a>.
</p><p>The <i><a href="/wiki/Muqatta%CA%BFat" title="MuqattaÊ¿at">MuqattaÊ¿at</a></i> (<a href="/wiki/Arabic" title="Arabic">Arabic</a>: <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1097707674" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="script-arabic script-Arab" dir="rtl" style="font-size: 125%;"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1097707674" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="script-arabic script-Arab" dir="rtl" style="font-size: 125%;">Ø­Ø±ÙÙ ÙÙØ·Ø¹Ø§Øª</span>â</span>â, <span title="Arabic-language romanization"><i lang="ar-Latn">á¸¥urÅ«f muqaá¹­á¹­aÊ¿Ät</i></span>, 'disjoined letters, disconnected letters';<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[92]</a></sup> also 'mysterious letters')<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Massey2005_105-0"><a href="#cite_note-Massey2005-105">[93]</a></sup> are combinations of between one and five <a href="/wiki/Arabic_alphabet" title="Arabic alphabet">Arabic letters</a> figuring at the beginning of 29 out of the 114 chapters of the Quran just after the basmala.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Massey2005_105-1"><a href="#cite_note-Massey2005-105">[93]</a></sup> The letters are also known as <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fawatih" title="Fawatih">fawÄtih</a></i> (<span title="Arabic-language text"><span dir="rtl" lang="ar">ÙÙØ§ØªØ­</span></span>), or 'openers', as they form the opening verse of their respective suras. Four surahs are named for their <span title="Arabic-language romanization"><i lang="ar-Latn">muqatta'at</i></span>: <a href="/wiki/Ta-Ha" title="Ta-Ha"><i>á¹¬ÄÊ¾-HÄÊ¾</i></a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ya_Sin" title="Ya Sin"><i>YÄÊ¾-SÄ«n</i></a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sad_(sura)" title="Sad (sura)"><i>á¹¢Äd</i></a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Qaf_(sura)" title="Qaf (sura)"><i> QÄf</i></a>. The original significance of the letters is unknown. <i><a href="/wiki/Tafsir" title="Tafsir">Tafsir</a></i> (<a href="/wiki/Exegesis" title="Exegesis">exegesis</a>) has interpreted them as abbreviations for either <a href="/wiki/Names_of_God_in_Islam" title="Names of God in Islam">names or qualities of God</a> or for the names or content of the respective surahs. According to <a href="/wiki/Rashad_Khalifa" title="Rashad Khalifa">Rashad Khalifa</a>, those letters are Quranic initials for a hypothetical mathematical code in the Quran, namely the <a href="/wiki/Quran_code" title="Quran code">Quran code</a> or known as Code 19.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[94]</a></sup>
</p><p>According to one estimate the Quran consists of 77,430 words, 18,994 unique words, 12,183 <a href="/wiki/Word_stem" title="Word stem">stems</a>, 3,382 <a href="/wiki/Lemma_(morphology)" title="Lemma (morphology)">lemmas</a> and 1,685 <a href="/wiki/Root_(linguistics)" title="Root (linguistics)">roots</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[95]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Contents">Contents</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/God_in_Islam" title="God in Islam">God in Islam</a>, <a href="/wiki/Prophets_and_messengers_in_Islam" title="Prophets and messengers in Islam">Prophets and messengers in Islam</a>, <a href="/wiki/Islamic_attitudes_towards_science" title="Islamic attitudes towards science">Islamic attitudes towards science</a>, <a href="/wiki/Biblical_and_Quranic_narratives" title="Biblical and Quranic narratives">Biblical and Quranic narratives</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Historical_reliability_of_the_Quran" title="Historical reliability of the Quran">Historical reliability of the Quran</a></div>
<p>The Quranic content is concerned with basic Islamic beliefs including the existence of <a href="/wiki/God_in_Islam" title="God in Islam">God</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Islamic_eschatology" title="Islamic eschatology">resurrection</a>. Narratives of the early <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Prophets_in_Islam" title="Prophets in Islam">prophets</a>, ethical and legal subjects, historical events of Muhammad's time, charity and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Salat" title="Salat">prayer</a> also appear in the Quran. The Quranic verses contain general exhortations regarding right and wrong and historical events are related to outline general moral lessons. Verses pertaining to natural phenomena have been interpreted by Muslims as an indication of the authenticity of the Quranic message.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-saeed_108-0"><a href="#cite_note-saeed-108">[96]</a></sup> The style of the Quran has been called "allusive," with commentaries needed to explain what is being referred toâ"events are referred to, but not narrated; disagreements are debated without being explained; people and places are mentioned, but rarely named."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Crone-2008_109-0"><a href="#cite_note-Crone-2008-109">[97]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Monotheism">Monotheism</span></h3>
<p>The central theme of the Quran is <a href="/wiki/Monotheism" title="Monotheism">monotheism</a>. God is depicted as living, eternal, omniscient and omnipotent (see, e.g., <span class="plainlinks">Quran  <a class="external text" href="https://quran.com/2?startingVerse=20" rel="nofollow">2:20</a>,  <a class="external text" href="https://quran.com/2?startingVerse=29" rel="nofollow">2:29</a>,  <a class="external text" href="https://quran.com/2?startingVerse=255" rel="nofollow">2:255</a></span>). God's omnipotence appears above all in his power to create. He is the creator of everything, of the heavens and the earth and what is between them (see, e.g., <span class="plainlinks">Quran  <a class="external text" href="https://quran.com/13?startingVerse=16" rel="nofollow">13:16</a>,  <a class="external text" href="https://quran.com/2?startingVerse=253" rel="nofollow">2:253</a>,  <a class="external text" href="https://quran.com/50?startingVerse=38" rel="nofollow">50:38</a>, etc.).</span> All human beings are equal in their utter dependence upon God, and their well-being depends upon their acknowledging that fact and living accordingly.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-watt_45-4"><a href="#cite_note-watt-45">[39]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-saeed_108-1"><a href="#cite_note-saeed-108">[96]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Quran_rzabasi4.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1811" data-file-width="4197" decoding="async" height="95" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/Quran_rzabasi4.JPG/220px-Quran_rzabasi4.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/Quran_rzabasi4.JPG/330px-Quran_rzabasi4.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/Quran_rzabasi4.JPG/440px-Quran_rzabasi4.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Quran_rzabasi4.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A 12th-century Quran manuscript at <a href="/wiki/Reza_Abbasi_Museum" title="Reza Abbasi Museum">Reza Abbasi Museum</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>The Quran uses <a href="/wiki/Cosmological_argument" title="Cosmological argument">cosmological</a> and contingency arguments in various verses without referring to the terms to prove the <a href="/wiki/Existence_of_God" title="Existence of God">existence of God</a>. Therefore, the universe is originated and needs an originator, and whatever exists must have a sufficient cause for its existence. Besides, the design of the universe is frequently referred to as a point of contemplation: "It is He who has created seven heavens in harmony. You cannot see any fault in God's creation; then look again: Can you see any flaw?"<span class="plainlinks"><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[98]</a></sup></span><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[99]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Eschatology">Eschatology</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Islamic_eschatology" title="Islamic eschatology">Islamic eschatology</a></div>
<p>The doctrine of the last day and <a href="/wiki/Eschatology" title="Eschatology">eschatology</a> (the final fate of the universe) may be considered the second great doctrine of the Quran.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-watt_45-5"><a href="#cite_note-watt-45">[39]</a></sup> It is estimated that approximately one-third of the Quran is eschatological, dealing with the afterlife in the next world and with the day of judgment at the end of time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[100]</a></sup> There is a reference to the afterlife on most pages of the Quran and belief in the afterlife is often referred to in conjunction with belief in God as in the common expression: "Believe in God and the last day."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-haleem_113-0"><a href="#cite_note-haleem-113">[101]</a></sup> A number of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sura" title="Sura">suras</a> such as 44, 56, 75, 78, 81 and 101 are directly related to the afterlife and its preparations. Some suras indicate the closeness of the event and warn people to be prepared for the imminent day. For instance, the first verses of Sura 22, which deal with the mighty earthquake and the situations of people on that day, represent this style of divine address: "O People! Be respectful to your Lord. The earthquake of the Hour is a mighty thing."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[102]</a></sup>
</p><p>
The Quran is often vivid in its depiction of what will happen at the end time. Watt describes the Quranic view of End Time:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-watt_45-6"><a href="#cite_note-watt-45">[39]</a></sup></p><blockquote><p>The climax of history, when the present world comes to an end, is referred to in various ways. It is 'the Day of Judgment,' 'the Last Day,' 'the Day of Resurrection,' or simply 'the Hour.' Less frequently it is 'the Day of Distinction' (when the good are separated from the evil), 'the Day of the Gathering' (of men to the presence of God) or 'the Day of the Meeting' (of men with God). The Hour comes suddenly. It is heralded by a shout, by a thunderclap, or by the blast of a trumpet. A cosmic upheaval then takes place. The mountains dissolve into dust, the seas boil up, the sun is darkened, the stars fall and the sky is rolled up. God appears as Judge, but his presence is hinted at rather than described.â¦ The central interest, of course, is in the gathering of all mankind before the Judge. Human beings of all ages, restored to life, join the throng. To the scoffing objection of the unbelievers that former generations had been dead a long time and were now dust and mouldering bones, the reply is that God is nevertheless able to restore them to life.</p></blockquote><p>The Quran does not assert a natural <a href="/wiki/Immortality" title="Immortality">immortality</a> of the human <a href="/wiki/Soul" title="Soul">soul</a>, since man's existence is dependent on the will of God: when he wills, he causes man to die; and when he wills, he raises him to life again in a bodily <a href="/wiki/Resurrection" title="Resurrection">resurrection</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rcmartin_115-0"><a href="#cite_note-rcmartin-115">[103]</a></sup>
</p><h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Prophets">Prophets</span></h3>
<p>According to the Quran, God communicated with man and made his will known through signs and revelations. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Prophets_in_Islam" title="Prophets in Islam">Prophets</a>, or 'Messengers of God', received revelations and delivered them to humanity. The message has been identical and for all humankind. "Nothing is said to you that was not said to the messengers before you, that your lord has at his Command forgiveness as well as a most Grievous Penalty."<span class="plainlinks"><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[104]</a></sup></span> The revelation does not come directly from God to the prophets. Angels acting as God's messengers deliver the divine revelation to them. This comes out in <span class="plainlinks">Quran  <a class="external text" href="https://quran.com/42?startingVerse=51" rel="nofollow">42:51</a></span>, in which it is stated: "It is not for any mortal that God should speak to them, except by revelation, or from behind a veil, or by sending a messenger to reveal by his permission whatsoever He will."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rippin_59-1"><a href="#cite_note-rippin-59">[52]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rcmartin_115-1"><a href="#cite_note-rcmartin-115">[103]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ethico-religious_concepts">Ethico-religious concepts</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Second_Sura_from_the_Qur%27an.jpg"><img alt="Verse about the month of Ramadan, second sura, verse 185. from a Quran manuscript dated to 1510" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="440" data-file-width="640" decoding="async" height="151" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/Second_Sura_from_the_Qur%27an.jpg/220px-Second_Sura_from_the_Qur%27an.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/Second_Sura_from_the_Qur%27an.jpg/330px-Second_Sura_from_the_Qur%27an.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/Second_Sura_from_the_Qur%27an.jpg/440px-Second_Sura_from_the_Qur%27an.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Second_Sura_from_the_Qur%27an.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Verse about the month of Ramadan, second sura, verse 185. from a Quran manuscript dated to <a href="/wiki/1510" title="1510">1510</a></div></div></div>
<p>Belief is a fundamental aspect of morality in the Quran, and scholars have tried to determine the semantic contents of "belief" and "believer" in the Quran.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-toshihiko_117-0"><a href="#cite_note-toshihiko-117">[105]</a></sup> The ethico-legal concepts and exhortations dealing with righteous conduct are linked to a profound awareness of God, thereby emphasizing the importance of faith, accountability, and the belief in each human's ultimate encounter with God. People are invited to perform acts of charity, especially for the needy. Believers who "spend of their wealth by night and by day, in secret and in public" are promised that they "shall have their reward with their Lord; on them shall be no fear, nor shall they grieve."<span class="plainlinks"><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[106]</a></sup></span> It also affirms family life by legislating on matters of marriage, divorce, and inheritance. A number of practices, such as usury and gambling, are prohibited. The Quran is one of the fundamental sources of Islamic law (<i><a href="/wiki/Sharia" title="Sharia">sharia</a></i>). Some formal religious practices receive significant attention in the Quran including the formal prayers (<i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Salat" title="Salat">salat</a></i>) and fasting in the month of <a href="/wiki/Ramadan" title="Ramadan">Ramadan</a>. As for the manner in which the prayer is to be conducted, the Quran refers to prostration.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jecampo_27-2"><a href="#cite_note-jecampo-27">[24]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rcmartin_115-2"><a href="#cite_note-rcmartin-115">[103]</a></sup> The term for charity, <i><a href="/wiki/Zakat" title="Zakat">zakat</a></i>, literally means purification. Charity, according to the Quran, is a means of self-purification.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tsonn_89-1"><a href="#cite_note-tsonn-89">[78]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[107]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Encouragement_for_the_sciences">Encouragement for the sciences</span></h3><p>
The astrophysicist <a href="/wiki/Nidhal_Guessoum" title="Nidhal Guessoum">Nidhal Guessoum</a>, while being highly critical of pseudo-scientific claims made about the Quran, has highlighted the encouragement for sciences that the Quran provides by developing "the concept of knowledge." He writes:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nidhal2011_120-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nidhal2011-120">[108]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 174">:â174â</span></sup></p><blockquote><p>The Qur'an draws attention to the danger of conjecturing without evidence (<i>And follow not that of which you have not the (certain) knowledge of...</i> 17:36) and in several different verses asks Muslims to require proofs (<i>Say: Bring your proof if you are truthful</i> 2:111), both in matters of theological belief and in natural science.</p></blockquote><p>Guessoum cites Ghaleb Hasan on the definition of "proof" according to the Quran being "clear and strong... convincing evidence or argument." Also, such a proof cannot rely on an <a href="/wiki/Argument_from_authority" title="Argument from authority">argument from authority</a>, citing verse 5:104. Lastly, both assertions and rejections require a proof, according to verse 4:174.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nidhal2011_120-1"><a href="#cite_note-Nidhal2011-120">[108]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 56">:â56â</span></sup> <a href="/wiki/Ismail_al-Faruqi" title="Ismail al-Faruqi">Ismail al-Faruqi</a> and <a href="/wiki/Taha_Jabir_Alalwani" title="Taha Jabir Alalwani">Taha Jabir Alalwani</a> are of the view that any reawakening of the Muslim civilization must start with the Quran; however, the biggest obstacle on this route is the "centuries old heritage of tafseer (exegesis) and other classical disciplines" which inhibit a "universal, epidemiological and systematic conception" of the Quran's message.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nidhal2011_120-2"><a href="#cite_note-Nidhal2011-120">[108]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 117â8">:â117â8â</span></sup> The philosopher <a href="/wiki/Muhammad_Iqbal" title="Muhammad Iqbal">Muhammad Iqbal</a>, considered the Quran's methodology and epistemology to be empirical and rational.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nidhal2011_120-3"><a href="#cite_note-Nidhal2011-120">[108]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 58â9">:â58â9â</span></sup>
</p><p>There are around 750 verses<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Avoid_weasel_words" title="Wikipedia:Avoid weasel words"><span title="The material near this tag possibly uses too vague attribution or weasel words. (December 2016)">which?</span></a></i>]</sup> in the Quran dealing with natural phenomena. In many of these verses the study of nature is <i>"encouraged and highly recommended"</i>, and historical Islamic scientists like <a href="/wiki/Al-Biruni" title="Al-Biruni">Al-Biruni</a> and <a href="/wiki/Al-Battani" title="Al-Battani">Al-Battani</a> derived their inspiration from verses of the Quran.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs additional references to reliable sources. (December 2016)">additional citation(s) needed</span></a></i>]</sup> <a href="/wiki/Mohammad_Hashim_Kamali" title="Mohammad Hashim Kamali">Mohammad Hashim Kamali</a> has stated that "scientific observation, experimental knowledge and rationality" are the primary tools with which humanity can achieve the goals laid out for it in the Quran.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nidhal2011_120-4"><a href="#cite_note-Nidhal2011-120">[108]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 63">:â63â</span></sup> <a href="/wiki/Ziauddin_Sardar" title="Ziauddin Sardar">Ziauddin Sardar</a> built a case for Muslims having developed the foundations of modern science, by highlighting the repeated calls of the Quran to observe and reflect upon natural phenomenon.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nidhal2011_120-5"><a href="#cite_note-Nidhal2011-120">[108]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 75">:â75â</span></sup>
</p><p>The physicist <a href="/wiki/Abdus_Salam" title="Abdus Salam">Abdus Salam</a>, in his Nobel Prize banquet address, quoted a well known verse from the Quran (67:3â4) and then stated: "This in effect is the faith of all physicists: the deeper we seek, the more is our wonder excited, the more is the dazzlement of our gaze."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nidhal2011_120-6"><a href="#cite_note-Nidhal2011-120">[108]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 131">:â131â</span></sup> One of Salam's core beliefs was that there is no contradiction between Islam and the discoveries that science allows humanity to make about nature and the universe. Salam also held the opinion that the Quran and the Islamic spirit of study and rational reflection was the source of extraordinary civilizational development.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nidhal2011_120-7"><a href="#cite_note-Nidhal2011-120">[108]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 132">:â132â</span></sup> Salam highlights, in particular, the work of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alhazen" title="Alhazen">Ibn al-Haytham</a> and <a href="/wiki/Al-Biruni" title="Al-Biruni">Al-Biruni</a> as the pioneers of empiricism who introduced the experimental approach, breaking with Aristotle's influence and thus giving birth to modern science. Salam was also careful to differentiate between metaphysics and physics, and advised against empirically probing certain matters on which "physics is silent and will remain so," such as the doctrine of "creation from nothing" which in Salam's view is outside the limits of science and thus "gives way" to religious considerations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nidhal2011_120-8"><a href="#cite_note-Nidhal2011-120">[108]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 134">:â134â</span></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Literary_style">Literary style</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Touba3.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1024" data-file-width="768" decoding="async" height="267" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/Touba3.jpg/200px-Touba3.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/Touba3.jpg/300px-Touba3.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/Touba3.jpg/400px-Touba3.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Touba3.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Boys studying Quran, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Touba,_Senegal" title="Touba, Senegal">Touba, Senegal</a></div></div></div>
<p>The Quran's message is conveyed with various literary structures and devices. In the original Arabic, the suras and verses employ <a href="/wiki/Phonetics" title="Phonetics">phonetic</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Theme_(literature)" title="Theme (literature)">thematic</a> structures that assist the audience's efforts to recall the message of the text. Muslims<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Words_to_watch#Unsupported_attributions" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Words to watch"><span title="The material near this tag possibly uses too-vague attribution or weasel words. (February 2010)">who?</span></a></i>]</sup> assert (according to the Quran itself) that the Quranic content and style is inimitable.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Issa_121-0"><a href="#cite_note-Issa-121">[109]</a></sup>
</p><p>The language of the Quran has been described as "rhymed prose" as it partakes of both poetry and prose; however, this description runs the risk of failing to convey the rhythmic quality of Quranic language, which is more poetic in some parts and more prose-like in others. Rhyme, while found throughout the Quran, is conspicuous in many of the earlier Meccan suras, in which relatively short verses throw the rhyming words into prominence. The effectiveness of such a form is evident for instance in Sura 81, and there can be no doubt that these passages impressed the conscience of the hearers. Frequently a change of rhyme from one set of verses to another signals a change in the subject of discussion. Later sections also preserve this form but the style is more expository.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[110]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[111]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Quranic text seems to have no beginning, middle, or end, its nonlinear structure being akin to a web or net.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Britannica_14-12"><a href="#cite_note-Britannica-14">[11]</a></sup> The textual arrangement is sometimes considered to exhibit lack of continuity, absence of any chronological or thematic order and repetitiousness.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125"><a href="#cite_note-125">[xiii]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[xiv]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Michael_Sells" title="Michael Sells">Michael Sells</a>, citing the work of the critic <a href="/wiki/Norman_O._Brown" title="Norman O. Brown">Norman O. Brown</a>, acknowledges Brown's observation that the seeming disorganization of Quranic literary expressionâits scattered or fragmented mode of composition in Sells's phraseâis in fact a literary device capable of delivering profound effects as if the intensity of the prophetic message were shattering the vehicle of human language in which it was being communicated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ApproachQuran_128-0"><a href="#cite_note-ApproachQuran-128">[114]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[115]</a></sup> Sells also addresses the much-discussed repetitiveness of the Quran, seeing this, too, as a literary device.
</p><p>A text is <a href="/wiki/Self-reference" title="Self-reference">self-referential</a> when it speaks about itself and makes reference to itself. According to Stefan Wild, the Quran demonstrates this <a href="/wiki/Metatextuality" title="Metatextuality">metatextuality</a> by explaining, classifying, interpreting and justifying the words to be transmitted. Self-referentiality is evident in those passages where the Quran refers to itself as revelation (<span title="Arabic-language romanization"><i lang="ar-Latn">tanzil</i></span>), remembrance (<i><a href="/wiki/Dhikr" title="Dhikr">dhikr</a></i>), news (<span title="Arabic-language romanization"><i lang="ar-Latn">naba'</i></span>), criterion (<span title="Arabic-language romanization"><i lang="ar-Latn">furqan</i></span>) in a self-designating manner (explicitly asserting its Divinity, "And this is a blessed Remembrance that We have sent down; so are you now denying it?"),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130"><a href="#cite_note-130">[116]</a></sup> or in the frequent appearance of the "Say" tags, when Muhammad is commanded to speak (e.g., "Say: 'God's guidance is the true guidance'," "Say: 'Would you then dispute with us concerning God?'"). According to Wild the Quran is highly self-referential. The feature is more evident in early Meccan suras.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[117]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Interpretation">Interpretation</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Tafsir" title="Tafsir">Tafsir</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:282px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Tapurian_Qur%27an_(Al-Kusar).PNG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="575" data-file-width="497" decoding="async" height="324" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/Tapurian_Qur%27an_%28Al-Kusar%29.PNG/280px-Tapurian_Qur%27an_%28Al-Kusar%29.PNG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/Tapurian_Qur%27an_%28Al-Kusar%29.PNG/420px-Tapurian_Qur%27an_%28Al-Kusar%29.PNG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/7/75/Tapurian_Qur%27an_%28Al-Kusar%29.PNG 2x" width="280"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Tapurian_Qur%27an_(Al-Kusar).PNG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An early interpretation of Sura 108 of the Quran</div></div></div>
<p>The Quran has sparked a huge body of commentary and explication (<span title="Arabic-language romanization"><i lang="ar-Latn">tafsir</i></span>), aimed at explaining the "meanings of the Quranic verses, clarifying their import and finding out their significance."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto1_132-0"><a href="#cite_note-auto1-132">[118]</a></sup>
</p><p>Tafsir is one of the earliest academic activities of Muslims. According to the Quran, Muhammad was the first person who described the meanings of verses for early Muslims.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133"><a href="#cite_note-133">[119]</a></sup> Other early exegetes included a few <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Companions_of_Muhammad" title="Companions of Muhammad">Companions of Muhammad</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Abu_Bakr" title="Abu Bakr">Abu Bakr</a>, <a href="/wiki/Umar" title="Umar">'Umar ibn al-Khattab</a>, <a href="/wiki/Uthman" title="Uthman">'Uthman ibn 'Affan</a>, Ê»<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ali_ibn_Abi_Talib" title="Ali ibn Abi Talib">Ali ibn Abi Talib</a>, <a href="/wiki/Abdullah_ibn_Masud" title="Abdullah ibn Masud">'Abdullah ibn Mas'ood</a>, Ê»<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Abdullah_ibn_Abbas" title="Abdullah ibn Abbas">Abdullah ibn Abbas</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ubayy_ibn_Kab" title="Ubayy ibn Kab">Ubayy ibn KaÊ»b</a>, <a href="/wiki/Zayd_ibn_Thabit" title="Zayd ibn Thabit">Zayd ibn Thaabit</a>, <a href="/wiki/Abu_Musa_al-Ash%27ari" title="Abu Musa al-Ash'ari">Abu Moosaa al-Ashâari</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Abd_Allah_ibn_al-Zubayr" title="Abd Allah ibn al-Zubayr">âAbdullah ibn al-Zubayr</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134"><a href="#cite_note-134">[120]</a></sup> Exegesis in those days was confined to the explanation of literary aspects of the verse, the background of its revelation and, occasionally, interpretation of one verse with the help of the other. If the verse was about a historical event, then sometimes a few traditions (<i><a href="/wiki/Hadith" title="Hadith">hadith</a></i>) of Muhammad were narrated to make its meaning clear.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto1_132-1"><a href="#cite_note-auto1-132">[118]</a></sup>
</p><p>Because the Quran is spoken in <a href="/wiki/Classical_Arabic" title="Classical Arabic">classical Arabic</a>, many of the later converts to Islam (mostly non-Arabs) did not always understand the Quranic Arabic, they did not catch allusions that were clear to early Muslims fluent in Arabic and they were concerned with reconciling apparent conflict of themes in the Quran. Commentators erudite in Arabic explained the allusions, and perhaps most importantly, explained which Quranic verses had been revealed early in Muhammad's prophetic career, as being appropriate to the very earliest Muslim community, and which had been revealed later, canceling out or "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Naskh_(exegesis)" title="Naskh (exegesis)">abrogating</a>" (<span title="Arabic-language romanization"><i lang="ar-Latn">nÄsikh</i></span>) the earlier text (<span title="Arabic-language romanization"><i lang="ar-Latn">mansÅ«kh</i></span>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135"><a href="#cite_note-135">[121]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136"><a href="#cite_note-136">[122]</a></sup> Other scholars, however, maintain that no abrogation has taken place in the Quran.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137"><a href="#cite_note-137">[123]</a></sup>
</p><p>There have been several commentaries of the Quran by scholars of all denominations, popular ones include Tafsir <a href="/wiki/Ibn_Kathir" title="Ibn Kathir">ibn Kathir</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tafsir_al-Jalalayn" title="Tafsir al-Jalalayn">Tafsir al-Jalalayn</a>, <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Tafsir_Al_Kabir&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Tafsir Al Kabir (page does not exist)">Tafsir Al Kabir</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tafsir_al-Tabari" title="Tafsir al-Tabari">Tafsir al-Tabari</a>. More modern works of Tafisr include <a href="/wiki/Ma%27ariful_Qur%27an" title="Ma'ariful Qur'an">Ma'ariful Qur'an</a> written by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mufti_Muhammad_Shafi" title="Mufti Muhammad Shafi">Mufti Muhammad Shafi</a> and <a href="/wiki/Risale-i_Nur" title="Risale-i Nur">Risale-i Nur</a> by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bedi%C3%BCzzaman_Said_Nursi" title="BediÃ¼zzaman Said Nursi">BediÃ¼zzaman Said Nursi</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Esoteric_interpretation">Esoteric interpretation</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Esoteric_interpretation_of_the_Quran" title="Esoteric interpretation of the Quran">Esoteric interpretation of the Quran</a></div>
<p>Esoteric or <a href="/wiki/Sufism" title="Sufism">Sufi</a> interpretation attempts to unveil the inner meanings of the Quran. Sufism moves beyond the apparent (<span title="Arabic-language romanization"><i lang="ar-Latn">zahir</i></span>) point of the verses and instead relates Quranic verses to the inner or esoteric (<i><a href="/wiki/Batin_(Islam)" title="Batin (Islam)">batin</a></i>) and metaphysical dimensions of consciousness and existence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-alangodlas_138-0"><a href="#cite_note-alangodlas-138">[124]</a></sup> According to Sands, esoteric interpretations are more suggestive than declarative, they are allusions (<span title="Arabic-language romanization"><i lang="ar-Latn">isharat</i></span>) rather than explanations (<i><a href="/wiki/Tafsir" title="Tafsir">tafsir</a></i>). They indicate possibilities as much as they demonstrate the insights of each writer.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kristin_139-0"><a href="#cite_note-kristin-139">[125]</a></sup>
</p><p>Sufi interpretation, according to Annabel Keeler, also exemplifies the use of the theme of love, as for instance can be seen in Qushayri's interpretation of the Quran:
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r996844942">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>When Moses came at the appointed time and his Lord spoke to him, he asked, âMy Lord! Reveal Yourself to me so I may see You.â Allah answered, âYou cannot see Me! But look at the mountain. If it remains firm in its place, only then will you see Me.â When his Lord appeared to the mountain, He levelled it to dust and Moses collapsed unconscious. When he recovered, he cried, âGlory be to You! I turn to You in repentance and I am the first of the believers.â</p><div class="templatequotecite">ââ<cite> Quran <a class="external text" href="https://quran.com/7?startingVerse=143" rel="nofollow">7:143</a></cite></div></blockquote>
<p>Moses, in 7:143, comes the way of those who are in love, he asks for a vision but his desire is denied, he is made to suffer by being commanded to look at other than the Beloved while the mountain is able to see God. The mountain crumbles and Moses faints at the sight of God's manifestation upon the mountain. In Qushayri's words, Moses came like thousands of men who traveled great distances, and there was nothing left to Moses of Moses. In that state of annihilation from himself, Moses was granted the unveiling of the realities. From the Sufi point of view, God is the always the beloved and the wayfarer's longing and suffering lead to realization of the truths.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-keeler_140-0"><a href="#cite_note-keeler-140">[126]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Men_reading_the_Koran_in_Umayyad_Mosque,_Damascus,_Syria.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1875" data-file-width="2813" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/Men_reading_the_Koran_in_Umayyad_Mosque%2C_Damascus%2C_Syria.jpg/220px-Men_reading_the_Koran_in_Umayyad_Mosque%2C_Damascus%2C_Syria.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/Men_reading_the_Koran_in_Umayyad_Mosque%2C_Damascus%2C_Syria.jpg/330px-Men_reading_the_Koran_in_Umayyad_Mosque%2C_Damascus%2C_Syria.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/Men_reading_the_Koran_in_Umayyad_Mosque%2C_Damascus%2C_Syria.jpg/440px-Men_reading_the_Koran_in_Umayyad_Mosque%2C_Damascus%2C_Syria.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Men_reading_the_Koran_in_Umayyad_Mosque,_Damascus,_Syria.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Men reading the Quran at the Umayyad Mosque, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Damascus,_Syria" title="Damascus, Syria">Damascus, Syria</a></div></div></div>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Muhammad_Husayn_Tabatabaei" title="Muhammad Husayn Tabatabaei">Muhammad Husayn Tabatabaei</a> says that according to the popular explanation among the later exegetes, <span title="Arabic-language romanization"><i lang="ar-Latn">ta'wil</i></span> indicates the particular meaning a verse is directed towards. The meaning of revelation (<i><a href="/wiki/Tanzil" title="Tanzil">tanzil</a></i>), as opposed to <span title="Arabic-language romanization"><i lang="ar-Latn">ta'wil</i></span>, is clear in its accordance to the obvious meaning of the words as they were revealed. But this explanation has become so widespread that, at present, it has become the primary meaning of <span title="Arabic-language romanization"><i lang="ar-Latn">ta'wil</i></span>, which originally meant 'to return' or 'the returning place'. In Tabatabaei's view, what has been rightly called <span title="Arabic-language romanization"><i lang="ar-Latn">ta'wil</i></span>, or <a href="/wiki/Hermeneutics" title="Hermeneutics">hermeneutic</a> interpretation of the Quran, is not concerned simply with the denotation of words. Rather, it is concerned with certain truths and realities that transcend the comprehension of the common run of men; yet it is from these truths and realities that the principles of doctrine and the practical injunctions of the Quran issue forth. Interpretation is not the meaning of the verseârather it transpires through that meaning, in a special sort of transpiration. There is a spiritual realityâwhich is the main objective of ordaining a law, or the basic aim in describing a divine attributeâand then there is an actual significance that a Quranic story refers to.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ta'wil_141-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ta'wil-141">[127]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-The_Meaning_142-0"><a href="#cite_note-The_Meaning-142">[128]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:%D8%B9%DA%A9%D8%B3_%D9%87%D8%A7%DB%8C_%D9%85%D8%B1%D8%A7%D8%B3%D9%85_%D8%AA%D8%B1%D8%AA%DB%8C%D9%84_%D8%AE%D9%88%D8%A7%D9%86%DB%8C_%DB%8C%D8%A7_%D8%AC%D8%B2%D8%A1_%D8%AE%D9%88%D8%A7%D9%86%DB%8C_%DB%8C%D8%A7_%D9%82%D8%B1%D8%A7%D8%A6%D8%AA_%D9%82%D8%B1%D8%A2%D9%86_%D8%AF%D8%B1_%D8%A7%DB%8C%D8%A7%D9%85_%D9%85%D8%A7%D9%87_%D8%B1%D9%85%D8%B6%D8%A7%D9%86_%D8%AF%D8%B1_%D8%AD%D8%B1%D9%85_%D9%81%D8%A7%D8%B7%D9%85%D9%87_%D9%85%D8%B9%D8%B5%D9%88%D9%85%D9%87_%D8%AF%D8%B1_%D8%B4%D9%87%D8%B1_%D9%82%D9%85_20.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1333" data-file-width="2000" decoding="async" height="167" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/%D8%B9%DA%A9%D8%B3_%D9%87%D8%A7%DB%8C_%D9%85%D8%B1%D8%A7%D8%B3%D9%85_%D8%AA%D8%B1%D8%AA%DB%8C%D9%84_%D8%AE%D9%88%D8%A7%D9%86%DB%8C_%DB%8C%D8%A7_%D8%AC%D8%B2%D8%A1_%D8%AE%D9%88%D8%A7%D9%86%DB%8C_%DB%8C%D8%A7_%D9%82%D8%B1%D8%A7%D8%A6%D8%AA_%D9%82%D8%B1%D8%A2%D9%86_%D8%AF%D8%B1_%D8%A7%DB%8C%D8%A7%D9%85_%D9%85%D8%A7%D9%87_%D8%B1%D9%85%D8%B6%D8%A7%D9%86_%D8%AF%D8%B1_%D8%AD%D8%B1%D9%85_%D9%81%D8%A7%D8%B7%D9%85%D9%87_%D9%85%D8%B9%D8%B5%D9%88%D9%85%D9%87_%D8%AF%D8%B1_%D8%B4%D9%87%D8%B1_%D9%82%D9%85_20.jpg/250px-thumbnail.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/%D8%B9%DA%A9%D8%B3_%D9%87%D8%A7%DB%8C_%D9%85%D8%B1%D8%A7%D8%B3%D9%85_%D8%AA%D8%B1%D8%AA%DB%8C%D9%84_%D8%AE%D9%88%D8%A7%D9%86%DB%8C_%DB%8C%D8%A7_%D8%AC%D8%B2%D8%A1_%D8%AE%D9%88%D8%A7%D9%86%DB%8C_%DB%8C%D8%A7_%D9%82%D8%B1%D8%A7%D8%A6%D8%AA_%D9%82%D8%B1%D8%A2%D9%86_%D8%AF%D8%B1_%D8%A7%DB%8C%D8%A7%D9%85_%D9%85%D8%A7%D9%87_%D8%B1%D9%85%D8%B6%D8%A7%D9%86_%D8%AF%D8%B1_%D8%AD%D8%B1%D9%85_%D9%81%D8%A7%D8%B7%D9%85%D9%87_%D9%85%D8%B9%D8%B5%D9%88%D9%85%D9%87_%D8%AF%D8%B1_%D8%B4%D9%87%D8%B1_%D9%82%D9%85_20.jpg/375px-thumbnail.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/%D8%B9%DA%A9%D8%B3_%D9%87%D8%A7%DB%8C_%D9%85%D8%B1%D8%A7%D8%B3%D9%85_%D8%AA%D8%B1%D8%AA%DB%8C%D9%84_%D8%AE%D9%88%D8%A7%D9%86%DB%8C_%DB%8C%D8%A7_%D8%AC%D8%B2%D8%A1_%D8%AE%D9%88%D8%A7%D9%86%DB%8C_%DB%8C%D8%A7_%D9%82%D8%B1%D8%A7%D8%A6%D8%AA_%D9%82%D8%B1%D8%A2%D9%86_%D8%AF%D8%B1_%D8%A7%DB%8C%D8%A7%D9%85_%D9%85%D8%A7%D9%87_%D8%B1%D9%85%D8%B6%D8%A7%D9%86_%D8%AF%D8%B1_%D8%AD%D8%B1%D9%85_%D9%81%D8%A7%D8%B7%D9%85%D9%87_%D9%85%D8%B9%D8%B5%D9%88%D9%85%D9%87_%D8%AF%D8%B1_%D8%B4%D9%87%D8%B1_%D9%82%D9%85_20.jpg/500px-thumbnail.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:%D8%B9%DA%A9%D8%B3_%D9%87%D8%A7%DB%8C_%D9%85%D8%B1%D8%A7%D8%B3%D9%85_%D8%AA%D8%B1%D8%AA%DB%8C%D9%84_%D8%AE%D9%88%D8%A7%D9%86%DB%8C_%DB%8C%D8%A7_%D8%AC%D8%B2%D8%A1_%D8%AE%D9%88%D8%A7%D9%86%DB%8C_%DB%8C%D8%A7_%D9%82%D8%B1%D8%A7%D8%A6%D8%AA_%D9%82%D8%B1%D8%A2%D9%86_%D8%AF%D8%B1_%D8%A7%DB%8C%D8%A7%D9%85_%D9%85%D8%A7%D9%87_%D8%B1%D9%85%D8%B6%D8%A7%D9%86_%D8%AF%D8%B1_%D8%AD%D8%B1%D9%85_%D9%81%D8%A7%D8%B7%D9%85%D9%87_%D9%85%D8%B9%D8%B5%D9%88%D9%85%D9%87_%D8%AF%D8%B1_%D8%B4%D9%87%D8%B1_%D9%82%D9%85_20.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shia" title="Shia">Shia</a> Muslim girls reciting the Quran placed atop folding <a href="/wiki/Lectern" title="Lectern">lecterns</a> (<a href="/wiki/Rehal_(book_rest)" title="Rehal (book rest)"><i>rehal</i></a>) during <a href="/wiki/Ramadan" title="Ramadan">Ramadan</a> in <a href="/wiki/Qom" title="Qom">Qom</a>, <a href="/wiki/Iran" title="Iran">Iran</a></div></div></div>
<p>According to Shia beliefs, those who are firmly rooted in knowledge like Muhammad and the imams know the secrets of the Quran. According to Tabatabaei, the statement "none knows its interpretation except God" remains valid, without any opposing or qualifying clause.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-143"><a href="#cite_note-143">[129]</a></sup> Therefore, so far as this verse is concerned, the knowledge of the Quran's interpretation is reserved for God. But Tabatabaei uses other verses and concludes that those who are purified by God know the interpretation of the Quran to a certain extent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-The_Meaning_142-1"><a href="#cite_note-The_Meaning-142">[128]</a></sup>
</p><p>According to <a href="/wiki/Muhammad_Husayn_Tabatabai" title="Muhammad Husayn Tabatabai">Tabatabaei</a>, there are acceptable and unacceptable esoteric interpretations. Acceptable <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ta%27wil" title="Ta'wil">ta'wil</a></i> refers to the meaning of a verse beyond its literal meaning; rather the implicit meaning, which ultimately is known only to <a href="/wiki/God" title="God">God</a> and can't be comprehended directly through human thought alone. The verses in question here refer to the human qualities of coming, going, sitting, satisfaction, anger and sorrow, which are apparently attributed to <a href="/wiki/God_in_Islam" title="God in Islam">God</a>. Unacceptable <span title="Arabic-language romanization"><i lang="ar-Latn">ta'wil</i></span> is where one "transfers" the apparent meaning of a verse to a different meaning by means of a proof; this method is not without obvious inconsistencies. Although this unacceptable <span title="Arabic-language romanization"><i lang="ar-Latn">ta'wil</i></span> has gained considerable acceptance, it is incorrect and cannot be applied to the Quranic verses. The correct interpretation is that reality a verse refers to. It is found in all verses, the decisive and the ambiguous alike; it is not a sort of a meaning of the word; it is a fact that is too sublime for words. God has dressed them with words to bring them a bit nearer to our minds; in this respect they are like proverbs that are used to create a picture in the mind, and thus help the hearer to clearly grasp the intended idea.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-The_Meaning_142-2"><a href="#cite_note-The_Meaning-142">[128]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tabatabaee_144-0"><a href="#cite_note-Tabatabaee-144">[130]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="History_of_Sufi_commentaries">History of Sufi commentaries</span></h4>
<p>One of the notable authors of esoteric interpretation prior to the 12th century is Sulami (d. 1021) without whose work the majority of very early Sufi commentaries would not have been preserved. Sulami's major commentary is a book named <span title="Arabic-language romanization"><i lang="ar-Latn">Haqaiq al-Tafsir</i></span> ('Truths of Exegesis') which is a compilation of commentaries of earlier Sufis. From the 11th century onwards several other works appear, including commentaries by Qushayri (d. 1074), Daylami (d. 1193), Shirazi (d. 1209) and Suhrawardi (d. 1234). These works include material from Sulami's books plus the author's contributions. Many works are written in Persian such as the works of Maybudi (d. 1135) <span title="Arabic-language romanization"><i lang="ar-Latn">kashf al-asrar</i></span> ('the unveiling of the secrets').<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-alangodlas_138-1"><a href="#cite_note-alangodlas-138">[124]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Rumi" title="Rumi">Rumi</a> (d. 1273) wrote a vast amount of mystical poetry in his book <i><a href="/wiki/Masnavi" title="Masnavi">Mathnawi</a></i>. Rumi makes heavy use of the Quran in his poetry, a feature that is sometimes omitted in translations of Rumi's work. A large number of Quranic passages can be found in <span title="Arabic-language romanization"><i lang="ar-Latn">Mathnawi</i></span>, which some consider a kind of Sufi interpretation of the Quran. Rumi's book is not exceptional for containing citations from and elaboration on the Quran, however, Rumi does mention Quran more frequently.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jmojaddedi_145-0"><a href="#cite_note-jmojaddedi-145">[131]</a></sup> Simnani (d. 1336) wrote two influential works of esoteric exegesis on the Quran. He reconciled notions of God's manifestation through and in the physical world with the sentiments of Sunni Islam.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jelias_146-0"><a href="#cite_note-jelias-146">[132]</a></sup> Comprehensive Sufi commentaries appear in the 18th century such as the work of Ismail Hakki Bursevi (d. 1725). His work <span title="Arabic-language romanization"><i lang="ar-Latn">ruh al-Bayan</i></span> ('the Spirit of Elucidation') is a voluminous exegesis. Written in Arabic, it combines the author's own ideas with those of his predecessors (notably Ibn Arabi and <a href="/wiki/Al-Ghazali" title="Al-Ghazali">Ghazali</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jelias_146-1"><a href="#cite_note-jelias-146">[132]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Levels_of_meaning">Levels of meaning</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Quran_rzabasi1.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2394" data-file-width="2898" decoding="async" height="182" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Quran_rzabasi1.JPG/220px-Quran_rzabasi1.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Quran_rzabasi1.JPG/330px-Quran_rzabasi1.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Quran_rzabasi1.JPG/440px-Quran_rzabasi1.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Quran_rzabasi1.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>9th-century Quran in <a href="/wiki/Reza_Abbasi_Museum" title="Reza Abbasi Museum">Reza Abbasi Museum</a></div></div></div> <div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:IslamicGalleryBritishMuseum3.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2304" data-file-width="3072" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/IslamicGalleryBritishMuseum3.jpg/220px-IslamicGalleryBritishMuseum3.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/IslamicGalleryBritishMuseum3.jpg/330px-IslamicGalleryBritishMuseum3.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/IslamicGalleryBritishMuseum3.jpg/440px-IslamicGalleryBritishMuseum3.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:IslamicGalleryBritishMuseum3.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An 11th-century North African Quran at the <a href="/wiki/British_Museum" title="British Museum">British Museum</a></div></div></div>
<p>Unlike the Salafis and Zahiri, Shias and Sufis as well as some other <a href="/wiki/Islamic_philosophy" title="Islamic philosophy">Muslim philosophers</a> believe the meaning of the Quran is not restricted to the literal aspect.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-corbin1993_94-1"><a href="#cite_note-corbin1993-94">[83]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 7">:â7â</span></sup> For them, it is an essential idea that the Quran also has inward aspects. <a href="/wiki/Henry_Corbin" title="Henry Corbin">Henry Corbin</a> narrates a <i><a href="/wiki/Hadith" title="Hadith">hadith</a></i> that goes back to <a href="/wiki/Muhammad" title="Muhammad">Muhammad</a>:
</p>
<blockquote><p>The Quran possesses an external appearance and a hidden depth, an exoteric meaning and an esoteric meaning. This esoteric meaning in turn conceals an esoteric meaning (this depth possesses a depth, after the image of the celestial Spheres, which are enclosed within each other). So it goes on for seven esoteric meanings (seven depths of hidden depth).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-corbin1993_94-2"><a href="#cite_note-corbin1993-94">[83]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 7">:â7â</span></sup></p></blockquote>
<p>According to this view, it has also become evident that the inner meaning of the Quran does not eradicate or invalidate its outward meaning. Rather, it is like the soul, which gives life to the body.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-147"><a href="#cite_note-147">[133]</a></sup> Corbin considers the Quran to play a part in <a href="/wiki/Islamic_philosophy" title="Islamic philosophy">Islamic philosophy</a>, because <a href="/wiki/Gnosiology" title="Gnosiology">gnosiology</a> itself goes hand in hand with <a href="/wiki/Prophet#Islam" title="Prophet">prophetology</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-corbin1993_94-3"><a href="#cite_note-corbin1993-94">[83]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 13">:â13â</span></sup>
</p><p>Commentaries dealing with the <i><a href="/wiki/Zahir_(Islam)" title="Zahir (Islam)">zahir</a></i> ('outward aspects') of the text are called <span title="Arabic-language romanization"><i lang="ar-Latn">tafsir</i></span>, and hermeneutic and esoteric commentaries dealing with the <i><a href="/wiki/Batin_(Islam)" title="Batin (Islam)">batin</a></i> are called <i><a href="/wiki/Esoteric_interpretation_of_the_Quran" title="Esoteric interpretation of the Quran">ta'wil</a></i> ('interpretation' or 'explanation'), which involves taking the text back to its beginning. Commentators with an esoteric slant believe that the ultimate meaning of the Quran is known only to God.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Britannica_14-13"><a href="#cite_note-Britannica-14">[11]</a></sup> In contrast, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bi-la_kaifa" title="Bi-la kaifa">Quranic literalism</a>, followed by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Salafis" title="Salafis">Salafis</a> and <a href="/wiki/Zahiri" title="Zahiri">Zahiris</a>, is the belief that the Quran should only be taken at its apparent meaning.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Reappropriation">Reappropriation</span></h3>
<p>Reappropriation is the name of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hermeneutical" title="Hermeneutical">hermeneutical</a> style of some ex-Muslims who have converted to Christianity. Their style or reinterpretation can sometimes be geared towards <a href="/wiki/Apologetics" title="Apologetics">apologetics</a>, with less reference to the Islamic scholarly tradition that contextualizes and systematizes the reading (e.g., by identifying some verses as abrogated). This tradition of interpretation draws on the following practices: grammatical renegotiation, renegotiation of textual preference, retrieval, and concession.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-148"><a href="#cite_note-148">[134]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Translations">Translations</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Quran_translations" title="Quran translations">Quran translations</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_translations_of_the_Quran" title="List of translations of the Quran">List of translations of the Quran</a></div>
<p>Translating the Quran has always been problematic and difficult. Many argue that the Quranic text cannot be reproduced in another language or form.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-slate_149-0"><a href="#cite_note-slate-149">[135]</a></sup> Furthermore, an Arabic word may have a <a href="/wiki/Polysemy" title="Polysemy">range of meanings</a> depending on the context, making an accurate translation even more difficult.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_150-0"><a href="#cite_note-:2-150">[136]</a></sup>
</p><p>Nevertheless, the Quran has been <a href="/wiki/Translation" title="Translation">translated</a> into most <a href="/wiki/Africa" title="Africa">African</a>, <a href="/wiki/Asia" title="Asia">Asian</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Europe" title="Europe">European</a> languages.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-leaman_64-2"><a href="#cite_note-leaman-64">[56]</a></sup> The first translator of the Quran was <a href="/wiki/Salman_the_Persian" title="Salman the Persian">Salman the Persian</a>, who translated surat <i><a href="/wiki/Al-Fatiha" title="Al-Fatiha">al-Fatiha</a></i> into <a href="/wiki/Persian_language" title="Persian language">Persian</a> during the seventh century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-151"><a href="#cite_note-151">[137]</a></sup> Another translation of the Quran was completed in 884 in <a href="/wiki/Alwar" title="Alwar">Alwar</a> (<a href="/wiki/Sindh" title="Sindh">Sindh</a>, <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a>, now <a href="/wiki/Pakistan" title="Pakistan">Pakistan</a>) by the orders of Abdullah bin Umar bin Abdul Aziz on the request of the Hindu Raja Mehruk.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-152"><a href="#cite_note-152">[138]</a></sup>
</p><p>The first fully attested complete translations of the Quran were done between the 10th and 12th centuries in <a href="/wiki/Persian_language" title="Persian language">Persian</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Samanid_Empire" title="Samanid Empire">Samanid</a> king, <a href="/wiki/Mansur_I" title="Mansur I">Mansur I</a> (961â976), ordered a group of scholars from <a href="/wiki/Greater_Khorasan" title="Greater Khorasan">Khorasan</a> to translate the <a href="/wiki/Tafsir_al-Tabari" title="Tafsir al-Tabari">Tafsir al-Tabari</a>, originally in Arabic, into Persian. Later in the 11th century, one of the students of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Khwaja_Abdullah_Ansari" title="Khwaja Abdullah Ansari">Abu Mansur Abdullah al-Ansari</a> wrote a complete <i><a href="/wiki/Tafsir" title="Tafsir">tafsir</a></i> of the Quran in Persian. In the 12th century, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Abu_Hafs_Omar_al-Nasafi" title="Abu Hafs Omar al-Nasafi">Najm al-Din Abu Hafs al-Nasafi</a> translated the Quran into Persian.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-153"><a href="#cite_note-153">[139]</a></sup> The manuscripts of all three books have survived and have been published several times.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>Islamic tradition also holds that translations were made for Emperor Negus of Abyssinia and Byzantine Emperor <a href="/wiki/Heraclius" title="Heraclius">Heraclius</a>, as both <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Muhammad%27s_letters_to_the_Heads-of-State" title="Muhammad's letters to the Heads-of-State">received letters by Muhammad</a> containing verses from the Quran.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_150-1"><a href="#cite_note-:2-150">[136]</a></sup> In early centuries, the permissibility of translations was not an issue, but whether one could use translations in prayer.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>In 1936, translations in 102 languages were known.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_150-2"><a href="#cite_note-:2-150">[136]</a></sup> In 2010, the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/H%C3%BCrriyet_Daily_News_and_Economic_Review" title="HÃ¼rriyet Daily News and Economic Review">HÃ¼rriyet Daily News and Economic Review</a></i> reported that the Quran was presented in 112 languages at the 18th International Quran Exhibition in Tehran.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-154"><a href="#cite_note-154">[140]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Robert_of_Ketton" title="Robert of Ketton">Robert of Ketton</a>'s 1143 translation of the Quran for <a href="/wiki/Peter_the_Venerable" title="Peter the Venerable">Peter the Venerable</a>, <i><a href="/wiki/Lex_Mahumet_pseudoprophete" title="Lex Mahumet pseudoprophete">Lex Mahumet pseudoprophete</a></i>, was the first into a Western language (<a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-155"><a href="#cite_note-155">[141]</a></sup>
<a href="/wiki/Alexander_Ross_(writer)" title="Alexander Ross (writer)">Alexander Ross</a> offered the first English version in 1649, from the French translation of <i><a href="/wiki/L%27Alcoran_de_Mahomet" title="L'Alcoran de Mahomet">L'Alcoran de Mahomet</a></i> (1647) by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Andre_du_Ryer" title="Andre du Ryer">Andre du Ryer</a>. In 1734, <a href="/wiki/George_Sale" title="George Sale">George Sale</a> produced the first scholarly translation of the Quran into English; another was produced by <a href="/wiki/Richard_Bell_(Arabist)" title="Richard Bell (Arabist)">Richard Bell</a> in 1937, and yet another by <a href="/wiki/Arthur_John_Arberry" title="Arthur John Arberry">Arthur John Arberry</a> in 1955. All these translators were non-Muslims. There have been numerous translations by Muslims. Popular modern English translations by Muslims include The Oxford World Classic's translation by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Muhammad_Abdel_Haleem" title="Muhammad Abdel Haleem">Muhammad Abdel Haleem</a>, The Clear Quran by Dr Mustafa Khattab, <a href="/wiki/Sahih_International" title="Sahih International">Sahih International</a>'s translation, among various others.
</p><p>As with translations of the Bible, the English translators have sometimes favored archaic English words and constructions over their more modern or conventional equivalents; for example, two widely read translators, <a href="/wiki/Abdullah_Yusuf_Ali" title="Abdullah Yusuf Ali">Abdullah Yusuf Ali</a> and <a href="/wiki/Marmaduke_Pickthall" title="Marmaduke Pickthall">Marmaduke Pickthall</a>, use the plural and singular <i>ye</i> and <i>thou</i> instead of the more common <i><a href="/wiki/You" title="You">you</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-156"><a href="#cite_note-156">[142]</a></sup>
</p><p>The oldest <a href="/wiki/Gurmukhi" title="Gurmukhi">Gurmukhi</a> translation of the Quran Sharif has been found in village <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Lande,_Punjab&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Lande, Punjab (page does not exist)">Lande</a> of <a href="/wiki/Moga_district" title="Moga district">Moga district</a> of Punjab which was printed in 1911.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-157"><a href="#cite_note-157">[143]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-packed">
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 137.33333333333px"><div style="width: 137.33333333333px">
